The Twenty-Second Mixed Syllogism Conjunction
Author gives the twenty-second conjunction from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the twenty-second conjunction of mixed syllogisms and verify its validity using conversion of the consequent.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms combine categorical and conditional premises to yield a conditional conclusion.

This lesson unpacks the twenty-second conjunction, which pairs a particular affirmative categorical with a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative. By following the derivation step by step, you will see how the conclusion emerges and why the conversion of the consequent is key to the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The first premise is a particular affirmative categorical: 'Some B is C'. The second is a particular affirmative conditional: 'It may be that if D is R, then nothing of B is A' (i.e., no B is A).
2
Isolate the antecedent and consequent of the conditional
The antecedent is 'D is R'. The consequent is 'Nothing of B is A' (universal negative).
The conditional is particular affirmative because of 'it may be' (قد يكون).
3
Combine the categorical with the conditional consequent
Take the categorical 'Some B is C' and the conditional consequent 'No B is A'. From these, by categorical logic, we infer 'Some C is not A'. (Convert 'Some B is C' to 'Some C is B', then apply Ferio: No B is A, Some C is B → Some C is not A.)
This step uses simple conversion and first-figure categorical syllogism.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional with the same antecedent as the premise conditional, and the newly derived consequent: 'It may be that if D is R, then some C is not A'.
The conclusion is particular affirmative because the antecedent is possible (قد يكون).
5
Understand the explanation via 'aks al-tālī'
The author says 'its explanation is by conversion of the consequent' (بيانه بعكس التالي). This means the consequent of the conditional is transformed (contraposed or otherwise manipulated) to enable the combination with the categorical premise. Here, the conversion likely refers to contraposing 'No B is A' to 'No A is B' or similar, but the core idea is that the consequent is adjusted to fit the syllogistic pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني والعشرون: من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية، تاليها كلي سالب كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ب" ) ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") وبيانه بعكس التالي.
The twenty-second conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'It may be that if D is R, then nothing of B is A.' Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely: 'It may be that if D is R, then some C is not A.' Its explanation is by conversion of the consequent.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a mixed syllogism where both premises are particular: a particular affirmative categorical 'Some B is C' and a particular affirmative conditional 'It may be that if D is R, then nothing of B is A.' The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional with the same antecedent and a consequent derived by combining 'nothing of B is A' with 'some B is C' via categorical logic. The derived consequent is 'some C is not A' (since from 'no B is A' and 'some B is C', we get 'some C is not A' by first figure? Actually, the derivation likely uses conversion of the categorical premise to 'some C is B' and then application of the conditional consequent. The explanation (bayān) is 'by conversion of the consequent' (bi-aks al-tālī), meaning that the consequent of the conditional is converted (contraposed) to facilitate the syllogism.

Key Terms
جزئية موجبة
عكس التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The twenty-second conjunction shows how to derive a conditional conclusion from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional with a universal negative consequent, using conversion of the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mixed syllogism following the twenty-second conjunction using Islamic examples: categorical 'Some actions are recommended (مندوب)' and conditional 'It may be that if one intends reward, then no recommended action is done with pride (لا يعمل مندوب مع كبر)'. Derive the conclusion and verify the steps.
?
Identify the premises and conclusion in the following argument from fiqh: 'Some contracts are valid (صحيح). It may be that if there is coercion (إكراه), then no valid contract is enforceable (لا ينفذ عقد صحيح). Therefore, it may be that if there is coercion, then some valid contracts are not enforceable.' Does this match the twenty-second conjunction? Why or why not?

The Thirteenth Conjunction: Mixed Conditional and Categorical
Author gives the thirteenth conjunction from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional with a particular negative consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed syllogism from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional using qalb al-muttasila and the necessary consequent.

Core Idea
The thirteenth conjunction transforms a negative conditional into an affirmative to enable a mixed syllogism with a categorical.

In this lesson, we examine a specific mixed syllogism from the Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: the thirteenth conjunction. It combines a particular affirmative categorical with a particular negative conditional. The conclusion is a conditional derived by first converting the negative conditional into an affirmative (qalb al-muttasila) and then taking the necessary consequent. This technique allows categorical and conditional premises to interact logically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The first premise is a particular affirmative categorical: 'Some B is C'. The second premise is a particular negative conditional: 'Not every time D is R, some B is not A'. Note the quantifiers: both are particular.
The conditional premise is negative because it negates a universal claim.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The conditional 'Not every time D is R, some B is not A' is a particular negative proposition. It asserts that there is at least one instance where if D is R, it is not the case that some B is not A. In other words, it is a negated universal conditional.
This is key for the transformation step.
3
Apply qalb al-muttasila
Convert the negative conditional into an affirmative conditional. The author says 'by converting the connected conditional into an affirmative' (قلب المتصلة موجبة). This transformation makes the conditional compatible with the categorical premise for syllogistic combination.
The exact form of the affirmative conditional depends on the logical equivalence; the author assumes it yields a universal affirmative conditional.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a new conditional: its antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional (D is R), and its consequent is the result of combining the categorical premise with the consequent of the original conditional. The resultant conclusion is 'Not every time D is R, then nothing of C is A' (a particular negative conditional).
The necessary consequent (لازم النتيجة) is obtained after the transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب كقولنا : (بعض "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" ليس "أ" ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة موجبة، وأخذ لازم النتيجة.
The thirteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'Not every time D is R, some B is not A.' Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely: 'Not every time D is R, then nothing of C is A.' Its explanation is by converting the connected conditional into an affirmative and then taking the necessary consequent.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage details a mixed syllogism with a particular affirmative categorical 'Some B is C' and a particular negative conditional 'Not every time D is R, some B is not A' (which is a particular negative because it negates the universal 'every time'). The conclusion is a particular negative conditional 'Not every time D is R, then nothing of C is A' (i.e., a particular negative). The method of derivation involves first converting the conditional into an affirmative (presumably by obversion or contraposition) to get a form where the syllogism with the categorical can proceed, then taking the necessary consequent from that transformation.

Key Terms
جزئية سالبة (juz'iyya sāliba): A particular negative proposition (e.g., 'some B is not A').
قلب المتصلة (qalb al-muttasila): Converting a connected conditional (muttasila) into an equivalent form, typically by contraposition. [Note: In pure conditional logic, 'qalb' may refer to transposition; here it is a specific operation to facilitate mixing with categorical.]
لازم النتيجة (lāzim al-natīja): The necessary consequent, i.e., the conclusion that follows necessarily from the premises after transformation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Universal affirmative
B) Universal negative
C) Particular affirmative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
The thirteenth conjunction demonstrates how a negative conditional can be transformed to interact with a categorical premise, yielding a new conditional conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Derive the conclusion using the method of qalb al-muttasila and the necessary consequent. Write the conclusion as a conditional proposition.

First Conjunction of the Fourth Figure in Mixed Syllogisms
Author presents the first conjunction of the fourth figure for mixed syllogisms with two universal affirmative premises and a universal affirmative conditional consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a mixed syllogism from two universal affirmative premises in the fourth figure and explain the role of conversion of the consequent.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, the middle term is predicate of major and subject of minor; conversion yields the conclusion.

In this lesson, we explore the first conjunction of the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rābi'). The author presents a mixed syllogism combining a categorical universal affirmative premise with a conditional universal affirmative premise. Understanding how to derive the conditional conclusion requires applying the conversion of the consequent (aks al-tālī). This tool is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences where conditional reasoning and categorical classifications are combined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the categorical premise 'Every B is C' and the conditional premise 'Whenever D is R, then every A is B.'
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term is B, which appears as predicate of the categorical premise's subject and as predicate of the conditional's consequent.
In the fourth figure, the middle term is predicate of the major premise (categorical) and subject of the minor premise (conditional).
3
Extract the internal categorical syllogism
From the conditional's consequent (every A is B) and the categorical premise (every B is C), derive a first-figure categorical syllogism: every A is B, every B is C, therefore every A is C.
4
Apply conversion of the consequent
Convert 'every A is C' to its converse 'some C is A' (aks al-tālī). This conversion is necessary to align with the fourth figure's properties.
Conversion changes the quantity from universal to particular.
5
Form the conditional conclusion
The conclusion has the same antecedent as the conditional premise and the converted consequent: 'Whenever D is R, then some C is A.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين والتالي من الشرطية كلي موجب كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كليـة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحملية، وهي دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") وبيانه بعكس التالي.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmative [premises] and the consequent of the conditional is universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Whenever D is R, then every A is B.' Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of combining the consequent with the categorical, namely: 'Whenever D is R, then some C is A.' Its explanation is by conversion of the consequent.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage introduces the mixed syllogisms for the fourth figure (shakl rābi'). It specifies that the conditions for productivity and properties follow those already established in categorical syllogisms. Here, the first conjunction has two universal affirmative premises: a categorical 'Every B is C' and a conditional 'Whenever D is R, then every A is B.' Note that in the conditional, the subject of the consequent is 'A' and its predicate is 'B', so the middle term 'B' appears in both premises. The conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional with the same antecedent 'Whenever D is R' and a consequent derived by combining the categorical 'Every B is C' with the conditional's consequent 'every A is B' via a categorical syllogism to yield 'some C is A' (since from 'every B is C' and 'every A is B', we get 'every A is C' by first figure, but here the author gives 'some C is A' after conversion? Actually, the explanation says 'by conversion of the consequent' (bi-aks al-tālī), meaning the consequent of the conditional is converted to make it align with the categorical premise. The derived consequent 'some C is A' is the converse of 'some A is C'.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع (al-shakl al-rābi')
كليتان موجبتان (kulliyatān mūjibatān)
عكس التالي (aks al-tālī)
قياس مختلط (qiyās mukhtaliṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first conjunction of the fourth figure yields a universal affirmative conditional conclusion whose consequent is the converse of the categorical combination's result.

The 13th Conjunction: Mixing Categorical and Conditional Premises
Author describes the 13th conjunctive syllogism combining a categorical and a conditional premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate the 13th conjunctive syllogism by converting the conditional and taking the necessary consequence.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine premise types; their validity is proven by converting conditionals.

In this lesson, we examine the 13th valid conjunction of compound syllogisms. It uniquely pairs a categorical particular affirmative premise with a conditional particular negative premise whose consequent is particular negative. The resulting conclusion is a particular negative conditional. We will walk through the logical structure and the proof method described by the author, which involves converting the conditional to its affirmative equivalent and deriving the necessary implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
The first premise is a particular affirmative categorical: 'Some B is C'. The second premise is a particular negative conditional: 'Not every D is R such that some B is not A'. Note that the conditional's consequent is itself a particular negative categorical.
The variables (B, C, D, R, A) are placeholders; in real arguments, they stand for terms in Islamic sciences.
2
Understand the conclusion structure
The conclusion is a particular negative conditional: 'Not every D is R such that no C is A'. Its antecedent is the same as the conditional premise's antecedent ('Not every D is R'), and its consequent is the result of conjoining the categorical premise with the conditional's consequent: from 'Some B is C' and 'some B is not A' we infer 'no C is A' (i.e., all C are not A). This step requires logical manipulation.
The conclusion is not directly obvious; the proof is needed to show its validity.
3
Apply the proof: convert the conditional
The author says the proof is by qalb al-muttaṣila (conversion of the conditional) to affirmative, and akhdh lāzim al-natīja (taking the necessary consequence). First, convert the negative conditional (particular) into an affirmative universal conditional. The original 'Not every D is R such that some B is not A' becomes 'Every D is R such that no B is A'? Actually, careful: converting a particular negative conditional to a universal affirmative conditional is a known transformation. Then, combine with the categorical premise to derive the conclusion.
This conversion is a specific rule in Mantiq for conditional propositions.
4
Verify the conclusion follows
From the converted conditional and the categorical premise, the necessary consequence (lāzim) yields the conclusion. The result is that the conclusion's consequent 'no C is A' follows necessarily. Thus the syllogism is valid.
The precise logical steps are detailed in more advanced texts; here we focus on the conceptual structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي سالب كقولنا : (بعض "ب" "ج") و(ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" ليس "أ") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") وبيانه بقلب المتصلة موجبة، وأخذ لازم النتيجة.
The thirteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our statement: (Some B is C) and (Not every D is R such that some B is not A) and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not every D is R such that no C is A). Its explication is by converting the conditional to affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the result.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage defines the 13th valid conjunction in the system of conjunctive syllogisms. It combines a particular affirmative categorical premise (Some B is C) with a particular negative conditional premise whose consequent is particular negative (Not every D is R such that some B is not A). The resulting conclusion is a particular negative conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise, and a consequent derived by conjoining the categorical premise with the conditional's consequent: Not every D is R such that no C is A. The proof (bayan) is shown by first converting the original conditional to its affirmative equivalent (i.e., turning it into a universal affirmative conditional) and then taking the necessary implication of the result. This ensures the valid derivation of the conclusion.

Key Terms
جزئية موجبة حَمْلية
جزئية سالبة شرطية
قلب المتصلة
لازم النتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 13th conjunctive syllogism is validated by converting the negative conditional to affirmative and then deriving the necessary consequence, showing how categorical and conditional premises combine.

First Three Conjunctions of the Fourth Figure
Author presents the first three conjunctions with premises, conclusions, and proof method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and prove the first three productive conjunctions of the fourth figure conjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
All three conjunctions use two universal affirmatives and proof by conversion of the consequent.

In this lesson, we examine the first three conjunctions of the fourth figure. Each has a universal affirmative categorical premise and a universal affirmative conditional premise, differing only in the consequent's quantity and quality. The conclusion is always a universal affirmative conditional derived by converting the consequent of the conditional premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Note the categorical premise (Every B is C) and the conditional premise (Always if D is R then every/some/no A is B). The categorical gives a relation between B and C.
The middle term is B.
2
Combine the consequent with the categorical
Replace B in the conditional's consequent with C using the categorical. For example, 'Every A is B' becomes 'Every A is C' after combining? Actually, the author states the conclusion's consequent is the result of 'conjoining the consequent with the categorical'. For the first conjunction, the consequent 'every A is B' combined with 'Every B is C' yields 'some C is A' after conversion.
Direct combination would give 'Every A is C', but the author's result is 'some C is A' due to conversion.
3
Apply the proof method
The proof is by 'aks al-tālī (conversion of the consequent). This means converting the consequent of the conclusion's conditional back to the original form to verify.
Conversion of the consequent is a logical operation specific to conditional statements.

"أ") وبيانه كبيان الاقتران الأول. الاقتران الثامن من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة And if the composition is on the pattern of the fourth figure, its conditions for production and its properties are as previously in the categoricals, and its productive conjunctions are twenty: The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is universal affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that every A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is A). Its explication is by conversion of the consequent. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that no A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is not A). Its explication is like the previous. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that some A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is A). Its explication is by conversion of the consequent. The fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then some A is not B) and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then no C is A). Its explication is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then conversion of the consequent, then taking the necessary consequence of the result. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then some A is B) and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then every C is A). Its explication is like the previous. The sixth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then no A is B) and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then no C is A). Its explication is by converting the negative to affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the result. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (It may be if D is R then every A is B) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some C is A). Its explication is like the explication of the first conjunction. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (It may be if D is R then no A is B) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some C is not A). Its explication is like the explication of the second conjunction. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is particular... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the first nine productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) of the fourth figure in conjunctive syllogisms, analogous to the fourth figure in categorical syllogisms. The conditions and properties of this figure are as previously established for categoricals. There are twenty productive conjunctions total; here we have the first nine. Each conjunction is described with its premises (a categorical and a conditional), its conclusion (a conditional), and its proof method (bayan). The proof typically involves conversion of the consequent ('aks al-tali) or conversion of the conditional to affirmative (qalb al-muttasila) along with taking the necessary consequence. The first three conjunctions all have two universal affirmative premises, differing in the quantity and quality of the conditional's consequent (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative). The fourth through sixth have a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, with consequents of varying quantity and quality. The seventh through ninth have a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional. The ninth is incomplete due to the page break. The pattern shows the variety of valid combinations, each with a tailored proof.

قرائن منتجة

الاقترانات الثلاثة الأولى في الشكل الرابع تستخدم مقدمتين كليتين موجبتين مع اختلاف في تالي الشرطية، وبرهانها بعكس التالي.

?
حول النتيجة في الاقتران الثاني إلى صيغة إيجابية ثم طبق عكس التالي كما في البرهان.

Conjunctions 4-6: Universal Negative Conditional Premises
Author presents conjunctions 4-6 with their premises, conclusions, and a multi-step proof method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and prove the fourth to sixth productive conjunctions of the fourth figure, which involve a universal negative conditional premise.

Core Idea
When the conditional premise is universal negative, the proof requires converting it to affirmative first.

In this lesson, we cover conjunctions 4 to 6. Here the categorical premise remains universal affirmative, but the conditional premise is universal negative. The conclusion is a universal negative conditional. The proof method involves converting the conditional to affirmative, then conversion of the consequent, and finally taking the necessary consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The categorical is 'Every B is C', and the conditional is 'Not at all if D is R then (some/no A is B)'. Note the conditional is a universal negative.
The form 'ليس البتة إذا كان د ر ف...' means 'It is not the case that if D is R then ...' which is equivalent to a universal negative.
2
Convert the negative conditional to affirmative
The first step in the proof is 'qalb al-muttasila ila al-ijab' (converting the conditional to affirmative). This means transforming the universal negative conditional into an equivalent universal affirmative conditional.
For example, 'Not at all if D is R then some A is not B' becomes 'Always if D is R then no A is B'? Actually, careful: the text says 'قلب المتصلة إلى الإيجاب' then 'عكس التالي' then 'أخذ لازم النتيجة'.
3
Apply conversion of the consequent and take necessary consequence
After converting to affirmative, apply 'aks al-tālī as before, then derive the necessary consequence (lāzim al-natīja) to get the final conclusion.
The necessary consequence is the part that follows logically.

Conjunctions 7-9: Particular Conditional Premises
Author presents conjunctions 7 and 8 (ninth incomplete) with their premises, conclusions, and proof method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and prove the seventh and eighth conjunctions of the fourth figure, involving a particular affirmative conditional premise, and note the incomplete ninth.

Core Idea
When the conditional premise is particular affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative conditional, and the proof mirrors the first two conjunctions.

In this lesson, we examine conjunctions 7 and 8 (the ninth is incomplete in the text). Here the categorical premise is universal affirmative, and the conditional premise is particular affirmative. The conclusions are particular affirmative conditionals. The proof method reverts to the simpler pattern of the first two conjunctions: conversion of the consequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Categorical: Every B is C. Conditional: It may be if D is R then (every/no A is B). Note the conditional is particular affirmative ('qad yakūn').
Particular affirmative conditionals use 'qad yakūn' (it may be) instead of 'dā'iman' (always).
2
Combine and convert
The conclusion's consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent of the conditional with the categorical, and the proof is by conversion of the consequent, exactly like the first conjunction for the seventh, and like the second for the eighth.
The seventh conjunction's consequent becomes 'some C is A', the eighth's becomes 'some C is not A'.
3
Note the incomplete ninth
The ninth conjunction is cut off in the source text. It would likely follow the pattern with a particular conditional and particular consequent.
We can infer it would have the same proof method.

And if the composition is on the pattern of the fourth figure, its conditions for production and its properties are as previously in the categoricals, and its productive conjunctions are twenty: The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is universal affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that every A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is A). Its explication is by conversion of the consequent. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that no A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is not A). Its explication is like the previous. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the consequent of the conditional is particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Always every D is R such that some A is B) and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always every D is R such that some C is A). Its explication is by conversion of the consequent. Its explication is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then conversion of the consequent, then taking the necessary consequence of the result. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then some A is B) and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then every C is A). Its explication is like the previous. The sixth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (Not at all if D is R then no A is B) and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then no C is A). Its explication is by converting the negative to affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the result. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (It may be if D is R then every A is B) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some C is A). Its explication is like the explication of the first conjunction. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our statement: (Every B is C) and (It may be if D is R then no A is B) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some C is not A). Its explication is like the explication of the second conjunction. The ninth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is particular... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the first nine productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) of the fourth figure in conjunctive syllogisms, analogous to the fourth figure in categorical syllogisms. The conditions and properties of this figure are as previously established for categoricals. There are twenty productive conjunctions total; here we have the first nine. Each conjunction is described with its premises (a categorical and a conditional), its conclusion (a conditional), and its proof method (bayan). The proof typically involves conversion of the consequent ('aks al-tali) or conversion of the conditional to affirmative (qalb al-muttasila) along with taking the necessary consequence. The first three conjunctions all have two universal affirmative premises, differing in the quantity and quality of the conditional's consequent (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative). The fourth through sixth have a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional, with consequents of varying quantity and quality. The seventh through ninth have a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional. The ninth is incomplete due to the page break. The pattern shows the variety of valid combinations, each with a tailored proof.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The General Pattern of Mixed Categorical-Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates fourteen conjunctions of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms with their conclusions and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism and derive its conclusion given the premises.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is that of the conditional premise.

This passage systematically enumerates fourteen valid conjunctions of a categorical premise (hamli) and a conditional premise (shartiyya). Although the list is long, all conjunctions follow a single pattern: the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is derived by conjoining the consequent of the conditional with the categorical premise. In this lesson, we will unpack this pattern using the first few conjunctions as examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the categorical and conditional premises in the Arabic. The categorical is a simple proposition (e.g., 'Every B is C'). The conditional has an antecedent after 'if' (إذا) and a consequent.
The categorical is always given first, then the conditional.
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent of the conditional
The antecedent is the part immediately after 'if' (e.g., 'D is R' in the examples). The consequent is the part after 'then' (e.g., 'some A is not B'). Note its quantity and quality.
The consequent can be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
3
Conjoin the consequent with the categorical premise
Take the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every B is C') and combine it with the consequent (e.g., 'some A is not B'). This produces a new proposition by substituting the subject of the categorical into the consequent? Actually, the text says 'result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical' – usually this means applying the categorical to the consequent's terms. For example, if the categorical is 'Every B is C' and the consequent is 'some A is not B', then conjoining yields 'some A is not C'? But the author gets 'no C is A'. This is a specific derivation related to conversion. For now, note that the categorical affects the consequent.
The exact method of conjoining is given in the bayan (proof) sections.
4
Form the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional, and the new proposition as its consequent. The quantity and quality of the conclusion match those of the original conditional (e.g., if the conditional was universal negative, the conclusion is also universal negative).
Check the text: 'Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional... namely ...'
5
Verify with the text
For the fourth conjunction, the premises are: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B). The conclusion given is (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A). Confirm that the antecedent is D is R and the consequent is no C is A.

الاقتران الثاني عشر من كليتين موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و (ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة شرطية، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ").
الاقتران الثالث عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة شرطية تاليها كلي سالب، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و (دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (دائماً كل ما كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ").
الاقتران الرابع عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة شرطية تاليها جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كليّة سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ")
The fourth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A). Its explanation is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then by converting the consequent, then by taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion.
The fifth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then every C is A). Its explanation is like that of what preceded it.
The sixth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A). Its explanation is by converting the negative to affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion.
The seventh conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then every A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is A). Its explanation is like that of the first conjunction.
The eighth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then no A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is not A). Its explanation is like that of the second conjunction.
The ninth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is A). Its explanation is like that of the third conjunction.
The tenth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, some A is not B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no C is A). Its explanation is as previously.
The eleventh conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, some A is B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, every C is A).
The twelfth conjunction [consists] of two universal affirmative categoricals and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no A is B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no C is A).
The thirteenth conjunction [consists] of a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Some B is C) and (Always, whenever D is R, no A is B). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always, whenever D is R, some C is not A).
The fourteenth conjunction [consists] of a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Some B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then every C is A).
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of fourteen valid mixed syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (hamli) with a conditional premise (shartiyya). The author is in the midst of defining the forms of syllogisms (ta'rif suwar al-hijaj) and here focuses on conjunctions (iqtiranat) that mix a categorical and a conditional. Each conjunction specifies: (1) the quantity and quality of the categorical premise (e.g., universal affirmative, particular affirmative); (2) the quantity and quality of the conditional premise (universal/particular, affirmative/negative); (3) the quantity and quality of the consequent of the conditional (the part after 'if'); (4) the resulting conclusion, which is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is derived from conjoining the consequent with the categorical; and (5) a brief indication of the proof method (bayan) such as conversion (qalb) or taking the necessary consequence (akhdh lazim al-natija). The conjunctions are numbered from fourth to fourteenth, implying that the first three were covered earlier. The patterns systematically cover all combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative for both premises, demonstrating a comprehensive treatment of mixed syllogisms. For example, the fourth conjunction takes a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is C') and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative ('It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B'), yielding a universal negative conditional conclusion. The explanation involves converting the conditional to affirmative, then converting the consequent, and taking the necessary consequence. This shows the logician's method for deriving valid conclusions from such mixed premises.

Checkpoint
In the fourth conjunction, the categorical premise is 'Every B is C' and the conditional premise is 'It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B'. What is the conclusion?
A) It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A.
B) It is always the case that if D is R, then some C is A.
C) It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then every C is A.
D) It may be that if D is R, then some C is not A.

Key Takeaway
Mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms always produce a conditional conclusion formed by conjoining the original antecedent with the result of combining the consequent and the categorical premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a categorical premise from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is haram' and a conditional premise: 'If a drink is made from grapes, then it is an intoxicant'. Using the pattern from this lesson, derive the conclusion. Check your answer against the structure of the fourth conjunction.
?
Identify the categorical premise, conditional premise, antecedent, consequent, and conclusion in the fourth conjunction from the Arabic text. Write them out using the same variable letters as the author.

Proof Methods for Mixed Conjunctions
Author enumerates fourteen conjunctions of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms with their conclusions and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the methods of conversion (qalb) and taking the necessary consequence (akhdh lazim al-natija) to prove the validity of mixed syllogisms.

Core Idea
The proofs for mixed conjunctions rely on converting conditionals and taking logical consequences.

The author not only lists the conjunctions but also indicates how each is proven. The explanations (bayan) refer to techniques such as converting the conditional to affirmative (qalb al-muttasila ila al-ijab), converting the consequent, and taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion (akhdh lazim al-natija). In this lesson, we will examine these proof methods using specific conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the bayan section for a conjunction
In the Arabic, find the part after 'wa bayanuhu' (and its explanation). For the fourth conjunction, it says 'wa bayanuhu bi qalb al-muttasila ila al-ijab, thumma bi 'aks al-tali, thumma bi akhdh lazim al-natija'.
Not all conjunctions have full explanations; some say 'its explanation is like that of what preceded it'.
2
Identify the first operation: converting the conditional to affirmative
The fourth conjunction's conditional is universal negative: 'It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B'. Converting it to affirmative means transforming it into a logically equivalent affirmative form, likely via contraposition or obversion. For example, the negative conditional 'not (if p then q)' is equivalent to 'p and not q'? But here it's more subtle. The author likely uses a known conversion rule: a universal negative conditional can be converted to a universal affirmative by some operation. We need to consider the logical form.
The exact operation is not spelled out here, but it is a standard step in the logical tradition.
3
Identify the second operation: converting the consequent
After converting the conditional to affirmative, the consequent of the original conditional (e.g., 'some A is not B') is also converted. 'Aks al-tali' means converting the consequent proposition. This might involve conversion (transposing subject and predicate) or some other transformation. The goal is to align the terms with the categorical premise.
Conversion of categorical propositions is a standard topic: 'some A is not B' converts to 'some B is not A'? Actually, particular negatives do not convert legitimately. So the context must specify which type of conversion is used.
4
Identify the third operation: taking the necessary consequence
'Akhdh lazim al-natija' means taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion. This likely involves deriving a new conclusion that logically follows from the result of the previous steps. For the fourth conjunction, after the first two steps, we have an affirmative conditional, and then we take its necessary consequence to obtain the final conclusion.
This step ensures the derived conclusion is logically entailed.
5
Apply to another conjunction
For the fifth conjunction, the text says 'its explanation is like that of what preceded it' (i.e., like the fourth). So the same three steps apply. Try to trace the proof for the fifth conjunction using the pattern from the fourth.
This shows that the proof method is systematic across conjunctions of the same type.

The fourth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A). Its explanation is by converting the conditional to affirmative, then by converting the consequent, then by taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion.
The fifth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then every C is A). Its explanation is like that of what preceded it.
The sixth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then no C is A). Its explanation is by converting the negative to affirmative and taking the necessary consequence of the conclusion.
The seventh conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then every A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is A). Its explanation is like that of the first conjunction.
The eighth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then no A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is not A). Its explanation is like that of the second conjunction.
The ninth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional, whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It may be that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be that if D is R, then some C is A). Its explanation is like that of the third conjunction.
The tenth conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, some A is not B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no C is A). Its explanation is as previously.
The eleventh conjunction [consists] of a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, some A is B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, every C is A).
The twelfth conjunction [consists] of two universal affirmative categoricals and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no A is B). Its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is not the case that whenever D is R, no C is A).
The thirteenth conjunction [consists] of a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, like our saying: (Some B is C) and (Always, whenever D is R, no A is B). Its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always, whenever D is R, some C is not A).
The fourteenth conjunction [consists] of a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Some B is C) and (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is B). Its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then every C is A).

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of fourteen valid mixed syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (hamli) with a conditional premise (shartiyya). The author is in the midst of defining the forms of syllogisms (ta'rif suwar al-hijaj) and here focuses on conjunctions (iqtiranat) that mix a categorical and a conditional. Each conjunction specifies: (1) the quantity and quality of the categorical premise (e.g., universal affirmative, particular affirmative); (2) the quantity and quality of the conditional premise (universal/particular, affirmative/negative); (3) the quantity and quality of the consequent of the conditional (the part after 'if'); (4) the resulting conclusion, which is always a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional and whose consequent is derived from conjoining the consequent with the categorical; and (5) a brief indication of the proof method (bayan) such as conversion (qalb) or taking the necessary consequence (akhdh lazim al-natija). The conjunctions are numbered from fourth to fourteenth, implying that the first three were covered earlier. The patterns systematically cover all combinations of universal/particular and affirmative/negative for both premises, demonstrating a comprehensive treatment of mixed syllogisms. For example, the fourth conjunction takes a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is C') and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative ('It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some A is not B'), yielding a universal negative conditional conclusion. The explanation involves converting the conditional to affirmative, then converting the consequent, and taking the necessary consequence. This shows the logician's method for deriving valid conclusions from such mixed premises.

Key Takeaway
The proofs of mixed conjunctions involve manipulating the conditional premise through conversion and deriving logical consequences.

?

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
The General Pattern of Mixed Categorical-Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates ten moods of mixed categorical-conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a mixed categorical-conditional syllogism and predict the structure of its conclusion.

Core Idea
The conclusion's antecedent is copied; the consequent is transformed by the categorical premise.

This lesson introduces the underlying principle behind the ten moods enumerated by the author. Despite the variety in quantity and quality, every mixed syllogism follows the same recipe: the conditional premise's antecedent becomes the conclusion's antecedent, and the categorical premise is applied to the conditional premise's consequent to produce the conclusion's consequent. Understanding this pattern is essential for recognising valid combinations in compound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Look for one categorical (ḥamliyya) and one conditional (sharṭiyya) premise. The categorical is a simple subject-predicate statement; the conditional is an if-then statement.
The conditional may be universal or particular, affirmative or negative.
2
Copy the antecedent of the conditional
The conclusion's antecedent is exactly the same as the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional premise. This does not change regardless of the categorical.
3
Combine the categorical with the consequent
Treat the categorical premise and the consequent (tālī) of the conditional as two premises of a categorical syllogism. Apply the rules of categorical syllogism to derive a new proposition. This becomes the conclusion's consequent.
If the categorical is universal negative, you may need to convert it first.
4
Form the conclusion
Write a conditional proposition using the antecedent from step 2 and the derived consequent from step 3. The quantifier and quality of the conclusion mirror those of the original conditional premise.
The conclusion always has the same modality (e.g., 'always', 'not every', 'it may be') as the conditional premise.

The tenth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as our saying: (Every B is J) and (Not every if D is R then some A is not B), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not every if D is R then no J is A), and its explanation is as previously. The eleventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as our saying: (Every B is J) and (Not every if D is R then some A is B), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not every if D is R then every J is A). The twelfth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as our saying: (Every B is J) and (Not every if D is R then no A is B), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not every if D is R then no J is A). The fourteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as our saying: (Some B is J) and (Not at all if D is R then some A is B), and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then every J is A). The fifteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal negative, as our saying: (Some B is J) and (It may be if D is R then no A is B), and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some J is not A). The sixteenth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is particular affirmative, as our saying: (Some B is J) and (Not every if D is R then some A is B), and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not every if D is R then every J is A). The seventeenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as our saying: (No B is J) and (Always if D is R then every A is B), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Always if D is R then no J is A), and its explanation is by conversion of the categorical. The eighteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is particular negative, as our saying: (No B is J) and (Not at all if D is R then some A is not B), and its conclusion is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (Not at all if D is R then some J is A), and its explanation is by conversion of the categorical. The nineteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is universal affirmative, as our saying: (No B is J) and (It may be if D is R then every A is B), and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely (It may be if D is R then some J is not A), and its explanation is by conversion of the categorical.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten moods (tenth to nineteenth) of mixed syllogisms that combine a categorical premise (hamliyya) with a conditional premise (shartiyya) to produce a conditional conclusion. Each mood is defined by the quantity and quality of the categorical premise (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative) and the type of conditional premise (affirmative/negative, universal/particular) and the quantity and quality of the consequent of the conditional. The conclusion always takes the form of a conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by combining the categorical premise with the consequent of the conditional, applying the rules of categorical syllogism. For example, in the tenth mood: from 'Every B is J' (categorical universal affirmative) and 'Not every if D is R then some A is not B' (conditional particular negative with particular negative consequent), we conclude 'Not every if D is R then no J is A'. The explanation follows the same pattern as previous moods: the categorical premise is used to operate on the consequent of the conditional via a kind of 'aqd' (conjunction). For moods that involve a universal negative categorical, the author notes that the explanation is by 'aks al-hamliyya' (conversion of the categorical), meaning the categorical is converted before combining. This systematic enumeration shows the author's thoroughness in covering all valid combinations of mixed syllogisms.

A mixed categorical-conditional syllogism yields a conditional conclusion whose antecedent is taken from the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by applying the categorical premise to the original consequent via categorical syllogism rules.

The Role of Conversion in Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains moods requiring conversion of the categorical premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion (aks) to a universal negative categorical premise in a mixed syllogism to derive the correct consequent.

Core Idea
When the categorical premise is universal negative, convert it before combining with the consequent.

In moods 17-19, the categorical premise is universal negative (e.g., 'No B is J'). The author explicitly notes that the explanation is 'by conversion of the categorical' (بيانه بعكس الحملية). This lesson focuses on that step: converting the universal negative into a form that can combine with the consequent to yield a valid conclusion. Understanding conversion is essential for handling these moods correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify a universal negative categorical
In moods 17-19, the categorical premise begins with 'لا شيء من' (No...is...). This is a universal negative (E proposition).
Example: لا شيء من ب ج (No B is J).
2
Convert the universal negative
Apply logical conversion (aks): a universal negative (E) converts to another universal negative? Actually, E converts to E. But the author uses it to change the subject and predicate? The author's intent: convert the categorical so that its predicate becomes subject, then combine. The exact conversion rule: 'No B is J' converts to 'No J is B' (simple conversion). This rearranges the terms for combination.
Conversion preserves truth: if 'No B is J' is true, then 'No J is B' is also true.
3
Combine the converted categorical with the consequent
Take the converted categorical (e.g., No J is B) as one premise and the consequent of the conditional (e.g., every A is B) as the other premise. Apply categorical syllogism rules to derive a new proposition. This becomes the conclusion's consequent.
For mood 17: converted 'No J is B' and original consequent 'every A is B' yield 'No A is J'? Actually, the derived consequent in the passage is 'no J is A'. So it's symmetric.
4
Form the conclusion
Write the conclusion as a conditional with the original antecedent and the derived consequent, preserving the quantifier of the conditional premise.
The conversion step is applied before the combination; the rest of the pattern remains the same.

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogisms with Conditional Premises
Author enumerates six compound syllogisms combining categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a compound syllogism consisting of a categorical and a conditional premise, using conversion of the categorical where required.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms with a conditional premise yield a conditional conclusion whose modality mirrors the conditional.

So far we have studied categorical syllogisms (both premises categorical) and conditional syllogisms (both premises conditional). Now we encounter compound syllogisms that mix a categorical premise with a conditional premise. This passage systematically presents six such patterns (conjunctions 15-20). The method is consistent: the conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent comes from the conditional premise and whose consequent is derived by conjoining the categorical with the consequent of the conditional, often after converting the categorical. Understanding this pattern allows you to handle mixed reasoning in Islamic sciences where a general rule (conditional) is applied to a specific case (categorical).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the categorical premise (حمليّة) and the conditional premise (شرطيّة متصلة). Note their quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).
The categorical is often about two terms (e.g., 'No B is J'). The conditional has an antecedent (مقدم) and a consequent (تالي).
2
Determine the consequent of the conditional
The conditional's consequent is itself a categorical proposition (e.g., 'every A is B'). This consequent will be combined with the given categorical.
The antecedent of the conditional (e.g., 'when D is R') will become the antecedent of the conclusion.
3
Combine the categorical with the consequent
Take the categorical premise (e.g., 'No B is J') and the consequent of the conditional (e.g., 'every A is B') and treat them as two premises to derive a new categorical conclusion. This often requires conversion of the categorical (عكس الحملية) to align the terms.
For example, from 'No B is J' and 'Every A is B', you can derive 'No A is J' by conversion of the categorical and then apply the first figure. But here the author directly states the result.
4
Form the conditional conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional proposition with the same antecedent as the original conditional and a new consequent: the result of step 3.
The modality (e.g., 'always' or 'may be') and quantity/quality of the conclusion match those of the original conditional.
5
Verify using conversion if needed
In some cases (conjunctions 17-20) the author explicitly mentions that demonstration (بيان) is by conversion of the categorical. Check that your derived consequent matches the given conclusion.
Conversion may be needed because the categorical's subject and predicate may not align with the consequent's terms.

The seventeenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, as we say: "No B is J" and "Always every when D is R, then every A is B" and its result is a universal affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent always: "Every when D is R, then no J is A" and its demonstration is by conversion of the categorical. The eighteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a universal negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, as we say: "No B is J" and "Not at all when D is R, then some ... is not B" and its result is a universal negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: "Not at all when D is R, then some J is A" and its demonstration is by conversion of the categorical. The nineteenth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative conditional whose consequent is a universal affirmative, as we say: "No B is J" and "It may be that when D is R, then every A is B" and its result is a particular affirmative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: "It may be that when D is R, then some J is not A" and its demonstration is by conversion of the categorical. The twentieth conjunction: from a universal negative categorical and a particular negative conditional whose consequent is a particular negative, as we say: "No B is J" and "Not every when D is R, then some A is not B" and its result is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, namely: "Not every when D is R, then every J is A" and its demonstration is by conversion of the categorical and inversion of the negative conditional into affirmation. And the results in all these conjunctions belong to the four figures, so they follow the conditional in absoluteness and modality.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage defines six compound syllogisms (iqṭirānāt) that combine a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) with a conditional premise (sharṭiyya). The author systematically lists conjunctions 15 through 20, each with a specific combination of quantity (universal/particular), quality (affirmative/negative), and the type of consequent in the conditional. The method is consistent: the conclusion is a conditional proposition whose antecedent is the antecedent of the given conditional premise, and whose consequent is derived by conjoining the consequent of the conditional with the categorical premise, often involving conversion of the categorical (ʿaks al-ḥamliyya). For example, in conjunction 17: from "No B is J" (universal negative categorical) and "Always every when D is R, then every A is B" (universal affirmative conditional with universal affirmative consequent), the conclusion is "Always every when D is R, then no J is A." The author notes that demonstration (bayān) is via conversion of the categorical, and at the end adds that for conjunction 20, inversion of the negative conditional into affirmation (qalb al-sāliba al-muttaṣila ilā al-ījāb) is also used. Finally, it is stated that the results follow the conditional in absoluteness (iṭlāq) and modality (jiha), meaning the modality of the conclusion mirrors that of the conditional premise (e.g., possible, necessary, etc.). This enumeration is part of a comprehensive treatment of compound syllogisms in traditional logic.

حملية
