Conclusion of the First Art: Particular Negative Conditional Result
Author describes the result of a conjunctive syllogism as a particular negative conditional and explains its derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a conjunctive syllogism's conclusion as a particular negative conditional and explain its derivation using conversion and inversion.

Core Idea
The result of a conjunctive syllogism is a particular negative conditional that follows the modality of its conditional premise.

This lesson explains the final form of the conclusion in a conjunctive syllogism. The author shows that the result is a particular negative conditional, derived by converting the categorical premise and inverting the negative conditional. Understanding this form is crucial for validating arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
A conjunctive syllogism has a conditional major premise (shartiyya muttasila) and a categorical minor premise (hamliyya). In the text, the conditional is of the form 'If D is R, then every J is A'.
2
Recognize the conclusion type
The conclusion is explicitly described as a 'particular negative conditional' (جزئية سالبة متصلة). Its antecedent is the antecedent of the original conditional, and its consequent is formed by conjoining the original consequent with the categorical premise.
The formula given is 'ليس كل ما كان د ر فكل ج أ'.
3
Trace the derivation using conversion and inversion
The derivation involves converting the categorical premise ('aks al-ḥamliyya) and inverting the negative conditional into an affirmative (qalb al-sāliba al-muttaṣila ilā al-ījāb). This yields the particular negative conditional conclusion.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth. Inversion of a conditional changes the order of negation.
4
Relate the conclusion's modality to the conditional
The author emphasizes that the results in all conjunctive syllogisms across the four figures follow the conditional in absoluteness (al-iṭlāq) and modality (al-jiha). So the conclusion's modal status (e.g., necessary, possible) is identical to that of the conditional major premise.
This safeguards against mistakenly assigning a different modality to the conclusion.

تتمة الفن الأول في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية 69 "ب" ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها مقدم الشرطية، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران التالي بالحَمْلية، وهي ليس كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ") وبيانه بعكس الحملية وقلب السالبة المتصلة إلى الإيجاب والنتائج في جميع هذه الاقترانات من الأشكال الأربعة فتابعة في الإطلاق والجهة للشرطية.
Completion of the first art on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. 69 'b': Its result is a particular negative conditional, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the categorical, and it is 'Not every A is such that if it is D then R, rather every J is A)' and its explanation by conversion of the categorical and inversion of the negative conditional to affirmation, and the results in all these conjunctions of the four figures follow the conditional in absoluteness and modality.

Commentary
This passage concludes a section on conjunctive syllogisms. It describes a specific result: a particular negative conditional (جزئية سالبة متصلة). The antecedent of this result is the antecedent of the original conditional premise, and its consequent comes from conjoining that consequent with the categorical premise. The phrase 'ليس كل ما كان د ر فكل ج أ' is a formula for the conditional. The explanation uses conversion (عكس) of the categorical and inversion (قلب) of the negative conditional into an affirmative. The author emphasizes that the results in all such conjunctions across the four figures follow the conditional in both absoluteness (الإطلاق) and modality (الجهة). This highlights that the modal status of the conclusion is derived from the conditional major premise. Islamic example: If we say 'Every time it rains, the ground is wet' (conditional universal affirmative), and 'It is raining' (categorical), the conclusion 'It is not the case that if it rains then the ground is not wet' is a particular negative conditional. The passage safeguards against misunderstanding that the conclusion might not follow the modality of the conditional.

The Five Conjunctions of Disjunctive and Categorical Premises
Author defines conditions and lists five productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct valid conjunctions of a categorical minor and a disjunctive major in the first figure pattern and identify the correct disjunctive conclusion.

Core Idea
Combining a categorical and a disjunctive yields a disjunctive conclusion; the categorical must be affirmative and the disjunctive universal.

In this passage, the author describes how to combine a categorical proposition (usually the minor) with a disjunctive proposition (usually the major) to produce a new disjunctive conclusion. This is a special kind of inference that follows the pattern of the first figure, but with a disjunctive major. The author presents five productive conjunctions, each with its own quantifier and quality. Understanding these conjunctions is essential for constructing valid arguments in disciplines like kalam and usul al-fiqh where disjunctive classifications are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the categorical
The categorical can be the minor or major premise. The passage focuses on when it is the minor. Determine if the categorical is the minor premise.
If it is the major, the analysis differs.
2
Determine the common term (sharika)
The common term between the categorical and the disjunctive must be the subject of the disjunctive and the predicate of the categorical. This sets up the first figure pattern.
The author calls this 'sharika' – the shared term.
3
Apply the conditions for production
The categorical must be affirmative, and the disjunctive must judge its subject universally (covering all parts). The conclusion will always be a disjunctive.
These conditions ensure validity.
4
Examine each of the five conjunctions
For each conjunction, note the quantity and quality of the categorical (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and the disjunctive, then see the resulting conclusion's form. Use the letter variables to track the pattern.
The first conjunction: both universal affirmative – yields universal affirmative disjunctive.
5
Verify with a concrete Islamic example
Replace the variables with terms from an Islamic science. For instance, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is either rational or irrational' yields 'Every human is either rational or irrational'.
This helps internalize the abstract pattern.

الفصل الرابع في الاقترانات الكائنة من المنفصلة والحَملية والحَمْلية إما مكان الصغرى وإما مكان الكبرى، فإن كانت مكان الصغرى فإما أن تكون الشركة بين الحَمْلية والمنفصلة في موضوع المنفصلة وإما في محمول أجزاء الانفصال، فإن كان الأول وهو أن يكون الاشتراك بينهما في موضوع المنفصلة فإما أن يكون موضوع المنفصلة محمولاً في الحَمْلية وإما موضوعاً، فإن كان محمولاً فيها فهو على نمط الشكل الأول، وشروطه في الإنتاج أن تكون الحَمْلية موجبة، والحكم بأجزاء الانفصال على موضوعها حكماً كلياً أي على كل أجزائه، إلا أن النتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا منفصلة، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وموضوع المنفصلة كلي، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "د" وإما "ر") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة، موضوع الحَمْلية فيها موضوع أجزاء الانفصال، وهي دائماً كل "ب" إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران الثاني من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة منفصلة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" دائماً ليس إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (كل "ب" دائماً ليس إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران الثالث من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (كل "ب" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران الرابع من كلية موجبة حملية وجزئية سالبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" ) و (كل "ج" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (كل "ب" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران الخامس من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة موجبة منفصلة كليّة الموضوع ، كقولنا: بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (دائماً بعض "ب" إما "د" وإما "ر").
The fourth chapter on the conjunctions composed of the disjunctive and the categorical. The categorical is either in the place of the minor or in the place of the major. If it is in the place of the minor, then either the commonality between the categorical and the disjunctive is in the subject of the disjunctive or in the predicate of the parts of the disjunction. If it is the first, i.e., the commonality between them is in the subject of the disjunctive, then either the subject of the disjunctive is a predicate in the categorical or a subject. If it is a predicate in it, then it is on the pattern of the first figure, and its conditions for production are that the categorical be affirmative, and the judgment about the parts of the disjunction on its subject is a universal judgment, i.e., on all its parts, except that the conclusion in it is only a disjunctive. Its productive conjunctions are eight: The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, and the subject of the disjunctive is universal, like our saying: (Every B is J) and (Always every J is either D or R) and its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive, the subject of the categorical in it is the subject of the parts of the disjunction, and it is always every B is either D or R. The second conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive, like our saying: (Every B is J) and (Every J is always not either D or R) so (Every B is always not either D or R). The third conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive, universal subject, like our saying: (Every B is J) and (Every J may be either D or R) so (Every B may be either D or R). The fourth conjunction from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive, universal subject, like our saying: (Every B is J) and (Every J may not be either D or R) so (Every B may not be either D or R). The fifth conjunction from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative disjunctive, universal subject, like our saying: Some B is J) and (Always every J is either D or R) so (Always some B is either D or R).
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth chapter on conjunctions of disjunctive and categorical propositions. The author first classifies the position of the categorical: it can be the minor or major premise. When it is the minor, the shared term (شركة) between the categorical and the disjunctive can be either the subject of the disjunctive or the predicate of its parts. If it is the subject of the disjunctive, then that subject might be the predicate or the subject of the categorical. When it is the predicate of the categorical, the conjunction follows the pattern of the first figure. Conditions: the categorical must be affirmative, and the judgment about the parts of the disjunction on its subject must be universal (covering all parts). The conclusion is always a disjunctive. There are eight productive conjunctions, of which the first five are given. (1) Both premises universal affirmative: e.g., 'Every B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R' yields 'Always every B is either D or R'. In Islamic terms: 'Every human is a male or female' and 'Every individual is human' implies 'Every individual is male or female'. (2) Universal affirmative categorical and universal negative disjunctive: e.g., 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is always neither D nor R' yields 'Every B is always neither D nor R'. (3) Universal affirmative categorical and particular affirmative disjunctive: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may be either D or R' yields 'Every B may be either D or R'. (4) Universal affirmative categorical and particular negative disjunctive: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may not be either D or R' yields 'Every B may not be either D or R'. (5) Particular affirmative categorical and universal affirmative disjunctive: 'Some B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R' yields 'Always some B is either D or R'. The author uses letters as variables; we can replace with concrete examples. This guards against incorrectly deriving a categorical conclusion when the major is disjunctive.

شركة
شكل أول

Key Takeaway
In the first figure pattern, combining an affirmative categorical minor with a universal disjunctive major yields a disjunctive conclusion that inherits the categorical's subject and the disjuncts.

Compound Syllogisms: Categorical Minor and First Figure Analogy
Author classifies compound syllogisms when categorical is minor and shares subject of disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid compound syllogism when the categorical premise is the minor and shares the subject of the disjunctive.

Core Idea
The categorical must be affirmative and the disjunctive universal for the conjunction to resemble the first figure.

In this lesson, we explore how a categorical premise (hamliyya) and a disjunctive premise (munfasila) can combine when the categorical is the minor. The author lays out the framework: the common term between them is the subject of the disjunctive, and when that subject is the predicate in the categorical, the mood mirrors the first figure. We'll learn the essential conditions for production and the nature of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions of the categorical premise
The author first distinguishes whether the categorical is the minor or the major premise. Here, focus on when it is the minor.
2
Determine the shared term between the two premises
The commonality (shareeka) can be in the subject of the disjunctive or in the predicate of its parts. This lesson focuses on sharing in the subject of the disjunctive.
3
Check the role of the shared term in the categorical
If the subject of the disjunctive is a predicate in the categorical, then the mood resembles the first figure. Otherwise, different patterns arise.
This is a condition for the first-figure analogy.
4
Apply the conditions for production
The categorical must be affirmative, and the disjunctive's judgment on its subject must be universal (covering all parts of the disjunction). Only then does the conjunction produce a conclusion.
5
Recognize the conclusion type
The conclusion is always a disjunctive proposition, and its modality (absoluteness, necessity, etc.) follows the modality of the disjunctive premise.
This holds regardless of the type of disjunction (exclusive, inclusive, etc.).

Chapter Four: On compound syllogisms composed of a disjunctive and a categorical premise. The categorical is either in the position of the minor premise or the major premise. If it is in the position of the minor, then the commonality between the categorical and the disjunctive is either in the subject of the disjunctive or in the predicate of the parts of the disjunction. If it is the first, i.e., the sharing is in the subject of the disjunctive, then either the subject of the disjunctive is a predicate in the categorical or a subject. If it is a predicate therein, then it is in the manner of the first figure, and its conditions for production are that the categorical be affirmative and the judgment on the parts of the disjunction concerning its subject be universal, i.e., concerning all its parts, except that the conclusion is only a disjunctive. Its productive conjunctions are eight: First conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the subject of the disjunctive is universal, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R,' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, the subject of the categorical being the subject of the parts of the disjunction, namely 'Always every B is either D or R.' Second conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is always not either D or R,' so 'Every B is always not either D or R.' Third conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may be either D or R,' so 'Every B may be either D or R.' Fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may not be either D or R,' so 'Every B may not be either D or R.' Fifth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R,' so 'Always some B is either D or R.' Sixth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always every J is not either D or R,' so 'Always some B is not either D or R.' Seventh conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J may be either D or R,' so 'Some B may be either D or R.' Eighth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J may not be either D or R,' so 'Some B may not be either D or R.' And the ruling on the conclusion in all, regarding absoluteness and modality, is the ruling of the disjunctive. It does not matter whether the disjunctive is true [exclusive] or 'manee' of both conjunction and void, or non-true 'manee' of conjunction without void, or of void without conjunction.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a classification of compound syllogisms formed from a categorical premise (hamliyya) and a disjunctive premise (munfasila). The author, likely al-Qazwini (the text is from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq), first distinguishes two cases: the categorical may be the minor or the major premise. Here, the categorical is the minor. Then there are two subcases based on the shared term (sharika) between the categorical and the disjunctive: either the shared term is the subject of the disjunctive or the predicate of its parts. The passage focuses on the first subcase where the shared term is the subject of the disjunctive. Within that, if the subject of the disjunctive is the predicate in the categorical (i.e., the categorical has the disjunctive's subject as its predicate), then the mood resembles the first figure of categorical syllogisms. The conditions for production are: the categorical must be affirmative, and the judgment of the disjunctive on its subject must be universal (i.e., covering all parts of the disjunction). The conclusion is always a disjunctive proposition (since one premise is disjunctive). The author then enumerates all eight productive conjunctions (qara'in muntija) based on the quantity and quality of the premises. The first four have a universal categorical minor, the last four have a particular categorical minor. Within each, the disjunctive can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Examples are given using letters B, J, D, R as terms. The conclusion's modality follows that of the disjunctive premise. Importantly, the type of disjunction (exclusive, inclusive, etc.) does not affect the validity of these compound moods. This is a detailed exposition of how to combine categorical and disjunctive logic in a manner analogous to the first figure.

The Eight Productive Conjunctions: Detailed Enumeration
Author lists eight productive conjunctions when categorical premise is the minor and shares subject of the disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate all eight productive conjunctions and generate the correct conclusion for each combination of quantity and quality of premises.

Core Idea
Eight conjunctions arise from varying the quantity and quality of the premises, each producing a disjunctive conclusion with the same modality as the disjunctive premise.

After establishing the conditions, the author lists all possible productive combinations when the categorical is the minor and the disjunctive's subject is its predicate. These eight conjunctions cover universal and particular categoricals, and universal/particular, affirmative/negative disjunctives. We will go through each one, understand the pattern, and see how the conclusion mirrors the disjunctive premise in modality.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two categories: universal categorical vs particular categorical
The first four conjunctions have a universal affirmative categorical (كل ب ج), the last four have a particular affirmative categorical (بعض ب ج). Note that the categorical is always affirmative as per conditions.
2
Identify the four disjunctive types within each category
Within each categorical strength, the disjunctive can be: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. That gives 4 x 2 = 8 conjunctions.
3
Work through the first conjunction
Both premises universal affirmative: categorical 'Every B is J' and disjunctive 'Always every J is either D or R'. The conclusion is 'Always every B is either D or R'. Note that the subject of the conclusion is the subject of the categorical (B), and the predicate is the disjunction (D or R).
Example: Every intoxicant is forbidden; always every forbidden thing is either a major sin or a minor sin → Every intoxicant is always either a major sin or a minor sin.
4
Apply the pattern to the remaining seven conjunctions
For universal negative disjunctive, the conclusion is universal negative disjunctive. For particular disjunctive, the conclusion is particular. The modality (always/may) follows the disjunctive premise.
The passage provides exact examples for each.
5
Note the ruling on the conclusion's modality
The conclusion's modality (absolute, necessary, possible) is the same as the disjunctive premise's modality. Also, the type of disjunction (exclusive, inclusive, etc.) does not affect the validity of these conjunctions.

Chapter Four: On compound syllogisms composed of a disjunctive and a categorical premise. The categorical is either in the position of the minor premise or the major premise. If it is in the position of the minor, then the commonality between the categorical and the disjunctive is either in the subject of the disjunctive or in the predicate of the parts of the disjunction. If it is the first, i.e., the sharing is in the subject of the disjunctive, then either the subject of the disjunctive is a predicate in the categorical or a subject. If it is a predicate therein, then it is in the manner of the first figure, and its conditions for production are that the categorical be affirmative and the judgment on the parts of the disjunction concerning its subject be universal, i.e., concerning all its parts, except that the conclusion is only a disjunctive. Its productive conjunctions are eight: First conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the subject of the disjunctive is universal, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R,' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, the subject of the categorical being the subject of the parts of the disjunction, namely 'Always every B is either D or R.' Second conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J is always not either D or R,' so 'Every B is always not either D or R.' Third conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may be either D or R,' so 'Every B may be either D or R.' Fourth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Every J may not be either D or R,' so 'Every B may not be either D or R.' Fifth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always every J is either D or R,' so 'Always some B is either D or R.' Sixth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Always every J is not either D or R,' so 'Always some B is not either D or R.' Seventh conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J may be either D or R,' so 'Some B may be either D or R.' Eighth conjunction: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Some B is J' and 'Every J may not be either D or R,' so 'Some B may not be either D or R.' And the ruling on the conclusion in all, regarding absoluteness and modality, is the ruling of the disjunctive. It does not matter whether the disjunctive is true [exclusive] or 'manee' of both conjunction and void, or non-true 'manee' of conjunction without void, or of void without conjunction.

Four Conjunctions of Categorical-Disjunctive (Third Figure)
Author begins listing twelve conjunctions when the disjunctive's subject is the categorical's subject, but only four are shown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the four valid conjunctions of a categorical and disjunctive premise when they share the subject in the third figure, and check their conclusions.

Core Idea
When both premises share the same subject, the compound syllogism follows the third figure and always yields a particular conclusion.

This passage extends the analysis of compound syllogisms to the case where the disjunctive and categorical share the same subject. This arrangement mirrors the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The passage lists four valid conjunctions, each differing in the quantity (universal/particular) of the disjunctive's subject and the type of disjunctive premise. Understanding these patterns is essential for constructing valid mixed syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term
Notice that in all four conjunctions, the term 'B' (ب) is the subject of both the categorical and the disjunctive. This is the middle term, analogous to the third figure.
2
Recognize the figure and conditions
The passage states that the mood is 'in the manner of the third figure' (على نمط الشكل الثالث). In categoricals, the third figure requires the middle term to be the subject of both premises, and the conclusion must be particular. Confirm that all four conclusions are particular (دائماً بعض or قد يكون بعض).
3
Distinguish the four conjunctions
Compare the first two conjunctions (both universal affirmative) with the last two (one universal categorical, one particular disjunctive). Note whether the disjunctive's subject is universal or particular, and how this affects the modality (دائماً vs. قد يكون) and the quantifier of the conclusion.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Replace the abstract letters with a concrete Islamic case. For example, take 'Every human (ب) is a creation (ج)' and 'Always every human (ب) is either mortal (د) or immortal (ر).' The conclusion is 'Always some creation (ج) is either mortal or immortal.' Check that the conclusion is particular and follows the pattern.
This example uses kalam concepts of creation and mortality.

Mixed Syllogisms: Categorical + Disjunctive Premises
Author lists three mixed syllogisms and explains modal rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid form of a mixed syllogism combining one categorical and one disjunctive premise and determine the modality of its conclusion.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's modality follows the disjunctive premise.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq presents three specific valid combinations of a categorical premise with a disjunctive premise. In each case, the subject of the disjunctive (C) serves as the middle term linking the categorical's predicate to its subject. More importantly, the author states a key rule: the conclusion's modality is determined by the disjunctive premise alone, regardless of the type of disjunction. This rule is essential for constructing and evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh, where disjunctive reasoning is used to exhaustively list possible rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their types
Find the categorical (hamliyya) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises. Note the quantifier (kulli/juz'i) and quality (positive/negative) of each.
The categorical premise is always particular affirmative in these examples.
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term is the term common to both premises. Here, it is the subject of the disjunctive (ج), which appears as predicate of the categorical.
The middle term ensures the two premises are connected.
3
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion has the same form as the disjunctive premise, but replaces its subject (ج) with the subject of the categorical (ب). The quantifier and modality of the conclusion are inherited from the disjunctive premise.
For example: 'Some B is C' + 'Every C is always not either D or R' gives 'Some B is always not either D or R'.
4
Apply the general rule
The author states that the conclusion's modality (jiha) is the ruling of the disjunctive premise, and this holds for all types of disjunctions (real, exclusive, etc.).
This rule is crucial for correct modal reasoning in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران السادس من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة منفصلة، كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج" ) و(دائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (دائماً بعض "ب" ليس إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران السابع من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع ، كقولنا : (بعض "ب" "ج " ) و(كل "ج" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ب" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر". الاقتران الثامن من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ج" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ب" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") وحكم النتيجة في الكل في ما يرجع إلى الإطلاق والجهة حكم المنفصلة، ولا تبالي كانت المنفصلة حقيقة أو مانعة من الجمع والخلو أو غير حقيقية مانعة منع الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع.
The sixth combination: from a particular affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive, with universal subject, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'Always, every C is not either D or R', so 'Always, some B is not either D or R'. The seventh combination: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive, with universal subject, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'Every C may be either D or R', so 'Some B may be either D or R'. The eighth combination: from a particular affirmative categorical and a particular negative disjunctive, with universal subject, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'Every C may not be either D or R', so 'Some B may not be either D or R'. And the ruling of the conclusion in all of them, regarding absoluteness and modality, is the ruling of the disjunctive, and it does not matter whether the disjunctive is real or exclusive (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww) or non-real, exclusive of collection without exclusion of void, or exclusive of void without collection.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents three specific valid combinations (iqitiranat) of mixed syllogisms where one premise is categorical (hamliyya) and the other is disjunctive (munfasila). The author uses placeholder variables (ب, ج, د, ر) to illustrate the logical forms.  Each combination varies the quantifier (particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of both premises, but importantly the subject of the disjunctive (ج) differs from the subject of the categorical (ب). After listing the three forms, the author draws a general rule: the conclusion's modality (i.e., whether it is necessary, absolute, possible, etc.) follows the modality of the disjunctive premise. Moreover, the type of disjunctive—whether it is a real disjunction (true exclusive or inclusive) or a non-real one (like man'at al-jam' only or man'at al-khuluw only)—does not affect the pattern; the same inference rules apply. This rule is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic, e.g., in fiqh reasoning: 'Some actions are obligatory. Every obligation is either prayer or fasting.' The conclusion 'Some actions are either prayer or fasting' follows with the same modality as the disjunctive 'every obligation is either prayer or fasting'.

In a mixed syllogism combining one categorical and one disjunctive premise, the conclusion follows the pattern of the disjunctive premise, and its modality is determined solely by that disjunctive premise.

Twelve Valid Mixed Syllogisms of the Third Figure
Enumerates twelve valid mixed syllogisms of the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the twelve productive mixed syllogisms of the third figure and verify their validity based on the shared subject condition.

Core Idea
Twelve and only twelve mixed syllogisms of the third figure are valid because the subject is shared.

This passage systematically lists all valid combinations of categorical and disjunctive premises in the third figure. The key condition is that the subject of the disjunctive must be the same as the subject of the categorical, making it the middle term. By varying quantity and quality, exactly twelve productive forms emerge, each with a schematic example. Understanding these forms ensures sound argumentation in disciplines like kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common subject
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Find the subject that appears in both the categorical and the disjunctive. The passage states: 'إذا كان موضوع المنفصلة هو موضوع الحمْلية'.
2
Classify premises by quantity and quality
For each combination, note the type of categorical (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and the disjunctive (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The author lists them in order: e.g., first is two universal affirmatives.
3
Note the subject of the disjunctive
The disjunctive premise may have a universal subject (كل ب) or a particular subject (بعض ب). This distinction affects the conclusion's quantifier.
4
Observe the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is always a particular disjunctive proposition (some ج is either D or R). Its modality matches that of the disjunctive premise (e.g., دائماً for eternal, قد يكون for possible).
5
Memorize the twelve combinations
The author asserts that only these twelve are productive. Commit them to memory to avoid invalid syllogisms in argumentation.

Mixed Syllogisms with Universal Affirmative Categorical (V–VIII)
Author lists the first four mixed categorical-disjunctive conjunctions (V to VIII) with their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion for any mixed syllogism combining a universal affirmative categorical premise with a negative disjunctive premise (universal or particular subject, with corresponding modality).

Core Idea
Universal affirmative categorical + negative disjunctive yields a particular negative disjunctive conclusion.

In the previous study of categorical and disjunctive propositions, we have seen their individual structures. Now, the author presents eight mixed conjunctions that combine one categorical and one disjunctive premise. This lesson focuses on the first four, where the categorical premise is universal affirmative and the disjunctive premise is negative. You will learn how the quantity and modality of the disjunctive premise determine the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The categorical premise is always universal affirmative: 'Every B is J'. Note the subject (B) and predicate (J).
The variable ب is the subject, ج is the predicate.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive premise is negative, either universal or particular, and has disjuncts D and R. Its modality is 'daiman' (always) for universal negative ('No B is either D or R') and 'qad' (may) for particular negative ('Some B may not be either D or R'). Its subject may be universal (كل ب) or particular (بعض ب).
3
Recognize the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is always a particular negative disjunctive: 'Some J is [modality] not either D or R'. The modality matches the disjunctive premise's modality (daiman or qad). The subject is 'some J', derived from the categorical's predicate.
The conclusion's subject is always particular because the categorical's predicate (J) is not distributed in a universal affirmative.
4
Verify with examples
In conjunction V (universal negative disjunctive with universal subject), the conclusion is 'Some J is always not either D or R'. In conjunction VII (particular negative disjunctive with universal subject), the conclusion is 'Some J may not be either D or R'. Confirm the pattern holds.
The subject of the disjunctive premise (كل ب or بعض ب) does not change the conclusion's quantity, which remains particular.

Commentary
This passage defines eight specific mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogisms, numbered from the fifth to the twelfth. Each conjunction consists of a categorical premise (حملیة) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة). The premises vary in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). The author gives for each a concrete example using letters as term variables (ب, ج, د, ر). The conclusion is always a disjunctive proposition whose subject is derived from the categorical's subject (ج) and whose disjuncts are the same as those of the disjunctive premise (د, ر). The modality (daim/قد) of the conclusion matches the modality of the disjunctive premise. For instance, in the fifth conjunction, the universal affirmative categorical 'Every B is J' combined with the universal negative disjunctive 'No B is either D or R' yields the particular negative disjunctive 'Some J is always not either D or R'. The author then notes that the method of proving these conjunctions is analogous to the proof for purely categorical syllogisms, and the conclusion inherits the modality of the disjunctive premise. This passage systematically catalogues possible valid combinations.

Mixed Syllogisms with Particular Affirmative Categorical (IX–XII)
Author lists the last four mixed categorical-disjunctive conjunctions (IX to XII) and adds a concluding remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion for any mixed syllogism combining a particular affirmative categorical premise with a disjunctive premise (affirmative or negative, universal or particular subject).

Core Idea
Particular affirmative categorical + any disjunctive yields a particular disjunctive conclusion.

The ninth through twelfth conjunctions shift the categorical premise from universal to particular affirmative. This change affects the quantity of the conclusion, but the pattern remains consistent: the conclusion is a particular disjunctive whose modality matches the disjunctive premise. The author also adds a concluding remark about the proof method and modality inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise
The categorical premise is always particular affirmative: 'Some B is J'. Note that the subject B is now particular (بعض ب).
The predicate J is undistributed, as before.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive premise may be affirmative or negative, universal or particular in its own subject (always universal subject here, i.e., كل ب), and with modality 'daiman' for universal affirmative/negative, or 'qad' for particular affirmative/negative.
In these four conjunctions, the disjunctive premise always has a universal subject (كل ب), but varies in quality and modality.
3
Recognize the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is a particular disjunctive: 'Always some J is either D or R' or 'Some J may be either D or R' depending on modality. It is always affirmative when the disjunctive is affirmative (IX, XI) and negative when the disjunctive is negative (X, XII). The modality matches the disjunctive premise's modality.
The conclusion's subject is 'some J' and its quantity is particular, just as before.
4
Verify with examples
In conjunction IX (universal affirmative disjunctive with 'daiman'), the conclusion is 'Always some J is either D or R'. In conjunction XI (particular affirmative disjunctive with 'qad'), the conclusion is 'Some J may be either D or R'. The pattern holds.
Compare with the previous lesson: the categorical's quantity (universal vs particular) does not change the conclusion's quantity — it remains particular in both cases.

الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وكليّة سالبة منفصلة كلية الموضوع كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" ) و(ليس البتة شيء من "ب" إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" دائماً ليس إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران السادس من كلية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة منفصلة جزئية الموضوع، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(بعض "ب" دائماً ليس إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" دائماً إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران السابع من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران الثامن من كليّة موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة منفصلة جزئية الموضوع كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج ") و (بعض "ب" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران التاسع من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية موجبة منفصلة، كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(دائماً كل "ب" إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (دائماً بعض "ج" إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران العاشر: من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وكلية سالبة منفصلة كلية الموضوع كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(ليس البتة شيء من "ب" إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" دائماً إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران الحادي عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كلية الموضوع، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" قد يكون إما "د" وإما "ر"). الاقتران الثاني عشر من جزئية موجبة حَمْلية وجزئية سالبة منفصلة، كليّة الموضوع، كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر") فـ (بعض "ج" قد لا يكون إما "د" وإما "ر"). وبيان الإنتاج في هذه الاقترانات على نحوه في الحَمْليات، وحكم النتيجة في الجهة والإطلاق حكم المنفصلة.
The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative categorical and a universal negative disjunctive universal subject, as our saying: (Every 'B' is 'J') and (Not at all any 'B' is either 'D' or 'R') so (Some 'J' is always not either 'D' or 'R'). The explanation of production in these conjunctions is similar to its way in the categoricals, and the ruling of the conclusion in modality and absolute is the ruling of the disjunctive.

Commentary
This passage defines eight specific mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogisms, numbered from the fifth to the twelfth. Each conjunction consists of a categorical premise (حملیة) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة). The premises vary in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). The author gives for each a concrete example using letters as term variables (ب, ج, د, ر). The conclusion is always a disjunctive proposition whose subject is derived from the categorical's subject (ج) and whose disjuncts are the same as those of the disjunctive premise (د, ر). The modality (daim/قد) of the conclusion matches the modality of the disjunctive premise. For instance, in the fifth conjunction, the universal affirmative categorical 'Every B is J' combined with the universal negative disjunctive 'No B is either D or R' yields the particular negative disjunctive 'Some J is always not either D or R'. The author then notes that the method of proving these conjunctions is analogous to the proof for purely categorical syllogisms, and the conclusion inherits the modality of the disjunctive premise. This passage systematically catalogues possible valid combinations.

Four Mixed Conjunctions of Universal Categorical and Disjunctive
Author gives corrected four conjunctions with universal categorical and notes four more for particular categoricals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four conjunctions of a universal categorical with an affirmative disjunctive and correctly produce the conclusion.

Core Idea
The conclusion is always a universal negative disjunction.

In this passage, the author corrects a textual error and presents the four valid forms of mixed syllogisms involving a universal categorical premise and an affirmative disjunctive premise. Understanding these forms is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with exclusive alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise type
Determine whether the categorical premise is universal affirmative (كل ب ج) or universal negative (لا شيء من ب ج).
2
Identify the disjunctive premise type
Determine whether the disjunctive premise is universal affirmative (دائماً إما بعض أ ج أو بعض د ج) or particular affirmative (قد يكون إما بعض أ ج أو بعض د ج).
The disjunctive is assumed exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw).
3
Derive the conclusion
For each combination, the conclusion is a disjunction of two universal negatives (لا شيء من ب أ أو لا شيء من ب د) with the same quantifier as the disjunctive premise.
If the disjunctive is universal, the conclusion is universal; if particular, the conclusion is particular.
4
Understand the proof
The proof relies on the exclusivity of the disjunctive: if some D is J, then nothing of A is J (and vice versa). Combined with the categorical, this yields the negative statements about B.
The author's explanation is analogous for all four conjunctions.

Commentary
This passage presents the corrected version of a set of mixed categorical-disjunctive syllogisms where the categorical premise is universal negative (or universal affirmative in the first two). The author notes that the original text contained an error from 'wa-amma in kana...' onward, and the correct version is given here. Four conjunctions are listed, numbered first to fourth. The first two use a universal affirmative categorical ('Every B is J') with a universal or particular affirmative disjunctive that has disjuncts involving 'some A is J' or 'some D is J'. The conclusion is a universal negative disjunctive: always nothing of B is A or nothing of B is D. The third and fourth use a universal negative categorical ('Nothing of B is J') with a universal or particular affirmative disjunctive, yielding similar conclusions. The explanation of the proof is analogous to the previous ones. Finally, the author notes that if the categorical premise is particular (presumably particular negative, since universal negative was just covered), there are four additional conjunctions, but their conclusion is an affirmative disjunctive with the subject being part of the categorical's subject. This passage is a critical correction of the earlier textual tradition.

Four Mixed Syllogisms with Categorical Major and Disjunctive Minor
Author enumerates four valid mixed syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid mixed syllogisms when the common term is the subject in the categorical and verify their conclusions.

Core Idea
When the common term is the subject in the categorical, the conclusion is a disjunction of two negative categoricals.

This passage introduces four valid combinations of a categorical premise and a disjunctive premise, a key pattern in Islamic logic for mixed syllogisms. Understanding these forms allows you to construct and evaluate arguments that combine categorical rules with disjunctive alternatives, a tool used in advanced theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term condition
The common term (here, 'C') must be the subject in the categorical premise. This places the syllogism in the fourth figure pattern. Confirm that the categorical has the form 'Every B is C' or 'No B is C', and the disjunctive has parts like 'some A is C' and 'some D is C'.
2
Classify the categorical and disjunctive
The categorical can be universal affirmative (Every B is C) or universal negative (No B is C). The disjunctive can be universal (daima) or particular (qad yakun), but must always be affirmative. Note the combinations: (1) universal affirm + universal disj, (2) universal affirm + particular disj, (3) universal neg + universal disj, (4) universal neg + particular disj.
3
Derive the conclusion pattern
For each valid combination, the conclusion is a disjunction of two negative universals: either no B is A or no B is D. The quality (universal/particular) of the conclusion matches the disjunctive premise: if the disjunctive is universal, the conclusion is universal; if particular, the conclusion is particular.
4
Verify with the demonstration
Take the first combination: from 'Every B is C' and 'Always either some A is C or some D is C'. Suppose some D is C: then (from the disjunctive) no A is C; with 'Every B is C', by Celarent we get 'No B is A'. Similarly, if some A is C, we get 'No B is D'. Thus the conclusion holds as a disjunction.
This reasoning uses two categorical syllogisms in the first figure.

الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما بعض "أ" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") ونتيجته (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د") لأنه إذا كان بعض "د" "ج" فلا شيء من "أ" "ج" وكان (كل "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإن كان بعض "أ" "ج" فلا شيء من "د" "ج" وكان (كل "ب" "ج" فلا شيء من "ب" "د"). الاقتران الثاني من كلية موجبة حملية وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إما بعض "أ" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") ونتيجته (قد يكون إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د") وبيانه كبيان الذي قبله. الاقتران الثالث من كلية سالبة حملية وكلية موجبة منفصلة كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما بعض "أ" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") ونتيجته (إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د") والبيان كالبيان. الاقتران الرابع من كلية سالبة حملية وجزئية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و(قد يكون إما بعض "أ" "ج" وإما بعض "د" "ج") ونتيجته (قد يكون إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د"). وإن كانت الحملية جزئية فقد يتركب منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخر أربعة، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون منفصلة موجبة موضوعها بعض موضوع الحملية والمحمول فيها كما سبق، إذا كانت المنفصلة.
The first combination: from two universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always either some A is C or some D is C', and its conclusion is 'Always either no B is A or no B is D', because if some D is C, then no A is C, and it was the case that every B is C, so no B is A; and if some A is C, then no D is C, and it was the case that every B is C, so no B is D. The second combination: from a universal affirmative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'It may be that either some A is C or some D is C', and its conclusion is 'It may be that either no B is A or no B is D', and its demonstration is like the demonstration of the one before it. The third combination: from a universal negative categorical and a universal affirmative disjunctive, like our saying: 'No B is C' and 'Always either some A is C or some D is C', and its conclusion is 'Either no B is A or no B is D', and the demonstration is like the demonstration. The fourth combination: from a universal negative categorical and a particular affirmative disjunctive, like our saying: 'No B is C' and 'It may be that either some A is C or some D is C', and its conclusion is 'It may be that either no B is A or no B is D'. And if the categorical is particular, then other four combinations may be composed from it with the disjunctive, except that the conclusion in them is an affirmative disjunctive whose subject is part of the subject of the categorical and whose predicate is as previously, if the disjunctive is ... end.

Commentary
This passage enumerates four valid mixed syllogisms where the common term between the categorical and the disjunctive is the subject in the categorical, placing it in the pattern of the fourth figure. The author first gives the general condition: when the common term is the subject in the categorical, the syllogism follows the fourth figure. Then he presents four specific iqtiran (combinations) varying the quantity and quality of the categorical premise and the disjunctive premise. The disjunctive premise is always affirmative, and its parts are uniform in quality (here, all are particular affirmatives). For each combination, he provides an example using letter variables (B, A, D for terms, C for the common term) and states the conclusion. The conclusion in each case is a disjunction of negatives (either no B is A or no B is D). The demonstration ('bayan') is given for the first combination and said to be similar for the others. Finally, he notes that if the categorical is particular, four additional combinations arise, but the conclusion will be an affirmative disjunctive. This passage is from a technical work on logic (daqaiq al-haqaiq) and requires careful parsing of the symbolic examples.

Conditions for Valid Mixed Syllogisms
Author states validity conditions for mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid mixed syllogism (categorical + disjunctive) and verify whether a given mixed syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
A mixed syllogism's validity hinges on the affirmative disjunctive and the universality or specificity of the categorical and disjunctive parts.

Mixed syllogisms combine a categorical premise (حملية) and a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) to yield a disjunctive conclusion. Not every combination is valid; the author lays down three precise conditions. This lesson extracts and explains those conditions, enabling you to check any mixed syllogism for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the categorical premise (e.g., 'Every B is C') and the disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Always either every D is B or every A is B').
2
Check the categorical premise
If the categorical is negative, it must be universal (كُلّية). A particular negative categorical invalidates the syllogism.
3
Check the disjunctive premise and its parts
The disjunctive must be affirmative (موجبة). If the categorical is particular affirmative, the disjunctive parts must be either universal negatives or particular affirmatives.
If the categorical is universal affirmative, no restriction on disjunctive parts is mentioned beyond the disjunctive being affirmative.
4
Apply to an example
Test the conditions on a sample syllogism, e.g., 'Every B is C' and 'Always either no D is B or no A is B'. The categorical is universal affirmative (ok), disjunctive is affirmative (ok), disjunctive parts are universal negatives (ok). So it satisfies conditions.

المختلطات منها، وشروطه في الإنتاج أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كليّة إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة إن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما كل "د" "ب" وإما كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة، جزئية الموضوع، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، كقولنا (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما لا شيء من "د" "ب" وإما لا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته إما) بعض "ج" ليس "د" وإما بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما بعض "د" "ب" وإما بعض "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الرابع من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات سالبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما ليس كل "د" "ب" وإما ليس كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "د" "ب" صدق كل "أ" "ب") وإن صدق ليس كل "أ" "ب" صدق كل "د" "ب" وهما مع الحَمْلية كما ذكرناه. وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة فقد تأتلف منها مع الحَمْلية إذا كانت موجبة أربعة اقترانات أخرى على هذا النمط، غير أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية منفصلة. وإن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة فقد تركب منها مع المنفصلة أربعة اقترانات أخرى إن كانت الحَمْلية كلية سالبة فقد تركب منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى، فعليك باعتبارها، وحكم النتيجة في الإطلاق والجهة فعلى ما تقدم في الحَمْليات. وأما إن كانت المنفصلة مكان الصغرى، والحَمْلية مكان الكبرى فالموضوع لأجزاء الانفصال إما أن يكون متحداً أو متعدداً، فإن كان متحداً فالحَمْلية إما أن تكون متحدة أو متعددة، فإن كانت متحدة فالمنفصلة إما حقيقية أو غير حقيقية، فإن كانت حقيقية بهيئة الحد الأوسط بينهما لا تخرج عن أن تكون على نمط الأشكال
The mixed ones from them, and their conditions for production are that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that the categorical be universal if it is negative, and that the parts of the disjunction be universal negatives, or particular affirmatives if the categorical is particular affirmative, and their productive combinations are sixteen combinations: The first combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always either every D is B or every A is B', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, particular in subject, namely 'Always either some C is D or some C is A'. The second combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always either no D is B or no A is B', and its conclusion is 'Either some C is not D or some C is not A'. The third combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always either some D is B or some A is B', and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Always either not every D is B or not every A is B', and its conclusion is like the first; because if 'not every D is B' is true, then 'every A is B' is true, and if 'not every A is B' is true, then 'every D is B' is true, and both with the categorical as we mentioned. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then four other combinations may be composed from it with the categorical if it is affirmative, in this pattern, except that the conclusion in them is a particular disjunctive. And if the categorical is particular affirmative, then four other combinations may be composed from it with the disjunctive; if the categorical is universal negative, then other combinations may be composed from it with the disjunctive. So it is upon you to consider them, and the ruling of the conclusion in absoluteness and modality is as previously in the categoricals. And as for if the disjunctive takes the place of the minor premise, and the categorical takes the place of the major premise, then the subject of the parts of the disjunction is either single or multiple; if it is single, then the categorical is either single or multiple; if it is single, then the disjunctive is either true or non-true; if it is true, in the form of the middle term between them, it does not exit from being in the pattern of the figures.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the general conditions for valid mixed syllogisms involving a categorical and a disjunctive premise, and then enumerates sixteen valid combinations (iqtiran) based on the quality and quantity of the disjunctive parts. The author begins by stating the conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative, the categorical must be universal if it is negative, and the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives if the categorical is particular affirmative. Then he lists four iqtiran in detail, each with the categorical universal affirmative and the disjunctive universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The conclusions vary accordingly. He then notes that further sets of four iqtiran arise when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, or when the categorical is particular affirmative or universal negative, totaling sixteen. The passage concludes with a remark about the case where the disjunctive is the minor premise and the categorical the major, discussing the unity or multiplicity of the subject and the nature of the disjunction. This is a technical enumeration typical of advanced logical works, requiring careful tracking of the variables.

Checkpoint
Which of the following satisfies the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism?
A) Categorical: 'Every B is C' (universal affirmative); Disjunctive: 'Always either no D is B or no A is B' (affirmative, parts universal negative).
B) Categorical: 'Some B is not C' (particular negative); Disjunctive: 'Always either every D is B or every A is B' (affirmative).
C) Categorical: 'Every B is C' (universal affirmative); Disjunctive: 'Not always either every D is B or every A is B' (negative disjunctive).
D) Categorical: 'Some B is C' (particular affirmative); Disjunctive: 'Always either no D is B or some A is B' (parts: universal negative and particular affirmative? But one part is negative, the other affirmative – condition says parts must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives, but mixed? The condition says 'كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة' meaning both parts should be of one type, not mixed. So this may be invalid.

Key Takeaway
A valid mixed syllogism requires an affirmative disjunctive, a universal categorical if negative, and uniform disjunctive parts (universal negative or particular affirmative) when the categorical is particular affirmative.

The Sixteen Productive Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates sixteen productive mixed syllogisms (iqtiran).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the sixteen productive mixed syllogisms a given combination belongs to and determine its conclusion.

Core Idea
Sixteen valid iqtiran are generated by varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts and the categorical premise.

After stating the conditions, the author enumerates sixteen productive combinations of mixed syllogisms, detailing four and implying the rest by analogy. This lesson walks through the four detailed ones and shows how the other twelve are derived, equipping you to recognise any combination and state its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categorical premise type
In the first four combinations, the categorical is universal affirmative (كل ب ج). Note its quality and quantity.
The text later mentions other cases where the categorical is particular affirmative or universal negative.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise and its parts
The disjunctive is always affirmative. Its parts can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The text gives one example for each in the first four iqtiran.
3
Match the pattern to one of the first four combinations
Combination 1: parts universal affirmative → conclusion 'Always either some C is D or some C is A'. Combination 2: parts universal negative → conclusion 'Either some C is not D or some C is not A'. Combination 3: parts particular affirmative → conclusion same as combo 1. Combination 4: parts particular negative → conclusion same as combo 1 (due to logical equivalence).
Combos 3 and 4 yield the same conclusion as combo 1.
4
Extend to other cases
If the disjunctive is particular affirmative, four more combinations follow analogously, with conclusions being particular disjunctives. If the categorical is particular affirmative, another four; if the categorical is universal negative, still another four, totaling sixteen.
The text says 'فعليك باعتبارها' – it is upon you to consider them, implying the reader can extrapolate.

Conditions for Mixed Syllogisms
Author defines conditions and enumerates first four productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism with a disjunctive major and categorical minor, and check whether a given conjunction satisfies them.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms require the disjunctive to be affirmative and the categorical to satisfy quantity conditions relative to the disjuncts.

Mixed syllogisms combine a disjunctive major premise with a categorical minor premise. The author sets three conditions for such a syllogism to produce a conclusion. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating arguments where a disjunctive choice is combined with a categorical fact, common in Islamic legal reasoning when ruling on alternative cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the disjunctive major (المنفصلة) and the categorical minor (الحَمْلية) in the syllogism. The disjunctive must be affirmative (موجبة) per the first condition.
2
Check the categorical's quantity and quality
If the categorical is negative, it must be universal (كليّة). If it is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. Apply this condition to your example.
3
Verify the disjunct parts
Ensure the parts (أجزاء الانفصال) are of the required quantity and quality as per the categorical: if categorical is particular affirmative, disjuncts must be universal negative or particular affirmative. Otherwise, no restriction beyond the categorical's own conditions.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' (categorical). 'Always either all nabidh is intoxicant or all wine is intoxicant' (disjunctive). The categorical is universal affirmative, disjunctive is affirmative, disjuncts are universal affirmatives. This satisfies conditions? Yes. Check if the conclusion follows.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختلطات منها، وشروطه في الإنتاج أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كليّة إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة إن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما كل "د" "ب" وإما كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة، جزئية الموضوع، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، كقولنا (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما لا شيء من "د" "ب" وإما لا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته إما بعض "ج" ليس "د" وإما بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما بعض "د" "ب" وإما بعض "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الرابع من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات سالبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما ليس كل "د" "ب" وإما ليس كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "د" "ب" صدق كل "أ" "ب") وإن صدق ليس كل "أ" "ب" صدق كل "د" "ب" وهما مع الحَمْلية كما ذكرناه.
Among them are the mixed syllogisms. The conditions for their production are that the disjunctive must be affirmative, that the categorical must be universal if it is negative, and that the parts of the disjunction must be either universal negatives or particular affirmatives if the categorical is particular affirmative. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being universal affirmatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either every D is B or every A is B'. Its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive, particular in subject, which is 'Always either some J is D or some J is A'. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being universal negatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either nothing of D is B or nothing of A is B'. Its result is 'Either some J is not D or some J is not A'. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being particular affirmatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either some D is B or some A is B'. Its result is like the first. The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being particular negatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either not every D is B or not every A is B'. Its result is like the first, because if 'Not every D is B' is true, 'Every A is B' is true; and if 'Not every A is B' is true, 'Every D is B' is true—both together with the categorical as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and enumerates the first four productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms where the disjunctive premise is the major and the categorical is the minor. The author begins by stating that the disjunctive must be affirmative. If the categorical is negative, it must be universal; if the categorical is particular affirmative, the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. There are sixteen productive conjunctions in total. The first four are given explicitly. In the first conjunction, both premises are universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmative. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive with a particular subject. An Islamic example: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden, and always either all nabidh is intoxicant or all wine is intoxicant' yields 'Always either some forbidden thing is nabidh or some forbidden thing is wine'. In the second conjunction, the parts are universal negatives, giving a particular negative disjunctive result. The fourth conjunction treats particular negative parts as equivalent to universal affirmative due to the categorical, as explained.

A) They must be universal affirmatives

C) They must be particular negatives

Key Takeaway
Mixed syllogisms with a disjunctive major require three conditions: affirmative disjunctive, universal categorical if negative, and proper quantity/quality of disjuncts if categorical is particular affirmative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

First Four Conjunctions of Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates first four productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms with universal affirmative categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the first four productive conjunctions of mixed syllogisms and produce the correct conclusion for each.

Core Idea
The first four conjunctions all have universal affirmative categoricals, but differ in the quantity/quality of the disjuncts, yielding different conclusion forms.

After stating the general conditions, the author enumerates the first four of sixteen productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms. Each conjunction assumes the categorical is universal affirmative, but the disjuncts vary between universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Understanding these patterns helps in constructing and evaluating such arguments encountered in Islamic dialectics and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the fixed categorical premise
In all four conjunctions, the categorical minor is universal affirmative (كل ب ج). This is a constant. Note that the subject of the categorical (ب) becomes the middle term linking the disjuncts.
2
Identify the disjunct type for each conjunction
For conjunctions 1, 2, 3, 4 the disjunct parts are respectively: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. These are the only variations. Check the Arabic phrases: كليات موجبة, كليات سالبة, جزئيات موجبة, جزئيات سالبة.
3
Determine the conclusion form
The conclusion is always a disjunctive with a particular subject (بعض ج). For conjunctions 1, 3, 4 the conclusion is 'either some J is D or some J is A' (affirmative). For conjunction 2, the conclusion is 'either some J is not D or some J is not A' (negative). Write them down.
Conjunction 4 gives the same conclusion as 1 because the particular negative disjuncts are treated as equivalent to universal affirmatives after conversion with the categorical.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take conjunction 2 example: categorical 'Every intoxicant is haram', disjunctive 'Always either nothing of nabidh is intoxicant or nothing of wine is intoxicant'. Conclusion: 'Either some haram is not nabidh or some haram is not wine'. This would be valid if the premises were true, but note the disjuncts are universal negatives, which is unusual in fiqh. Use a hypothetical: 'Every animal is mortal; always either no human is animal or no horse is animal' yields 'Either some mortal is not human or some mortal is not horse'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المختلطات منها، وشروطه في الإنتاج أن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون الحَمْلية كليّة إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات موجبة إن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة عشر اقتراناً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما كل "د" "ب" وإما كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة، جزئية الموضوع، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" "د" وإما بعض "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، كقولنا (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما لا شيء من "د" "ب" وإما لا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته إما بعض "ج" ليس "د" وإما بعض "ج" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما بعض "د" "ب" وإما بعض "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الرابع من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات سالبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(دائماً إما ليس كل "د" "ب" وإما ليس كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "د" "ب" صدق كل "أ" "ب") وإن صدق ليس كل "أ" "ب" صدق كل "د" "ب" وهما مع الحَمْلية كما ذكرناه.
Among them are the mixed syllogisms. The conditions for their production are that the disjunctive must be affirmative, that the categorical must be universal if it is negative, and that the parts of the disjunction must be either universal negatives or particular affirmatives if the categorical is particular affirmative. Its productive conjunctions are sixteen. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being universal affirmatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either every D is B or every A is B'. Its result is a universal affirmative disjunctive, particular in subject, which is 'Always either some J is D or some J is A'. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being universal negatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either nothing of D is B or nothing of A is B'. Its result is 'Either some J is not D or some J is not A'. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being particular affirmatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either some D is B or some A is B'. Its result is like the first. The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being particular negatives, such as our saying: 'Every B is J' and 'Always either not every D is B or not every A is B'. Its result is like the first, because if 'Not every D is B' is true, 'Every A is B' is true; and if 'Not every A is B' is true, 'Every D is B' is true—both together with the categorical as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions and enumerates the first four productive conjunctions for mixed syllogisms where the disjunctive premise is the major and the categorical is the minor. The author begins by stating that the disjunctive must be affirmative. If the categorical is negative, it must be universal; if the categorical is particular affirmative, the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. There are sixteen productive conjunctions in total. The first four are given explicitly. In the first conjunction, both premises are universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmative. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive with a particular subject. An Islamic example: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden, and always either all nabidh is intoxicant or all wine is intoxicant' yields 'Always either some forbidden thing is nabidh or some forbidden thing is wine'. In the second conjunction, the parts are universal negatives, giving a particular negative disjunctive result. The fourth conjunction treats particular negative parts as equivalent to universal affirmative due to the categorical, as explained.
