Mixed Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms: Conditions and Combinations
Sets conditions and combinatorial framework for mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two validity conditions for a mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogism and explain why sixteen combinations are considered.

Core Idea
Valid mixed syllogisms require an affirmative disjunctive and a universal categorical, producing a conclusion that prevents void alone.

This passage introduces the first figure of mixed syllogisms, where a disjunctive and a categorical premise combine. The author sets down explicit conditions for a valid conclusion and hints at a systematic enumeration of all possible productive combinations. Understanding this foundation is essential for mastering the formal structure of mixed arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The major premise is a disjunctive proposition (منفصلة) and the minor premise is a categorical proposition (حملية). In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the disjunctive and subject in the categorical.
Example: 'Every act is either obligatory or recommended' (disjunctive), 'This act is obligatory' (categorical).
2
State the conditions for validity
The disjunctive must be affirmative (إيجاب المنفصلة) and the categorical must be universal (كلية الحملية). These conditions ensure that the middle term is shared, as in categorical syllogisms.
If the categorical were particular, the middle term might not connect conclusively.
3
Understand the type of disjunction
The conclusion follows the type called 'mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'': the disjuncts cannot both be false, but they can both be true. This is a disjunction that prevents both being false but allows both to be true.
This distinguishes it from exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع والخلو).
4
Grasp the combinatorial enumeration
The author notes that considering the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the parts of the disjunction, there are sixteen possible productive combinations. These will be detailed later.
The sixteen come from 2 (quality) × 2 (quantity) for each disjunct part, multiplied across the two parts? Actually, the text says 'with respect to universality/particularity and positivity/negativity of the parts' — likely 2^4 = 16.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية الأربعة، فإن كان على نمط الشكل الأول بأن يكون محمولاً في المنفصلة وموضوعاً في الحَمْلية، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال متحدة الكيفية والكمية - وأنت تعرفه في المختلطات فيها - وله شروط وخواص، أما شروطه في الإنتاج فإيجاب المنفصلة، وكليّة الحَمْلية لتحقق الاشتراك في الحد الأوسط، كما تقدم في الحَمْليات، وأقسام خواصه تكون النتيجة فيها تابعة الخلو دون الجمع، وقرائنه المنتجة بالنظر إلى كليّة أجزاء الانفصال، وجزئياتها، وإيجابها، وسلبها، ستة عشر :اقتراناً
Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The four [figures], and if it is in the pattern of the first figure such that [the middle term] is predicate in the disjunctive and subject in the categorical, and let us assume the discussion when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity—and you know this in the mixed ones—and it has conditions and properties. As for its conditions for production: positivity of the disjunctive, and universality of the categorical to ensure shared middle term, as previously presented in categoricals. And the divisions of its properties: the conclusion therein follows the void alone and not the combination. And its productive combinations, considering the universality and particularity of the parts of the disjunction, and their positivity and negativity, are sixteen combinations.

Commentary
This passage introduces the study of mixed syllogisms composed of a disjunctive (munfasila) and a categorical (hamliyya) premise, specifically in the first figure. The author states that the middle term appears as predicate in the disjunctive and subject in the categorical. He assumes uniformity in quality (both affirmative or both negative) and quantity (both universal or both particular). The conditions for a valid syllogism are: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and the categorical must be universal, so that the middle term is shared. The resulting conclusion is of the type that prevents void but allows combination (man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'), meaning the disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. The author then notes that the total number of possible productive combinations, considering the different qualities and quantities of the disjunct parts, is sixteen. This sets the stage for enumerating these combinations.

First Combination of Disjunctive and Categorical Syllogism
Author presents the first valid combination: universal affirmative disjuncts and a universal affirmative categorical premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the first valid combination of a disjunctive and categorical premise and explain why it prevents void but not combination.

Core Idea
The first iqtiran shows a mixed syllogism where the conclusion prevents both false but allows both true.

This lesson unpacks the first valid mixed syllogism in the first figure. The author presents a disjunction with universal affirmative parts and a universal affirmative categorical premise, then demonstrates how the conclusion follows and what logical properties it has. Understanding this inference is crucial for distinguishing between exclusive and non-exclusive disjunctions in Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the disjunctive premise: 'Always every B is either C or D' (a universal affirmative disjunction). Find the categorical premise: 'Every D is A' (universal affirmative).
2
Understand the disjunction type
The disjunction is man'at al-khuluw duna al-jam' – it prevents both disjuncts being false but allows them to be true together.
This is the type of disjunction used in the conclusion.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since every B is either C or D, and every D is A, it follows that every B is either D or A. The conclusion is also a universal affirmative disjunction preventing void.
The conclusion does not prevent it being the case that some B is both D and A.
4
Analyze why combination is not prevented
The author notes that A might be more general than D, so what is true of D (being A) could also be true of C if A includes C. Thus, it's possible for a B to be both D and A (if it falls under A via C).
This is why the conclusion only prevents void, not combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً كل "ب" إما "ج" وإما "د") و(كل "د" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وهي دائماً لا يخلو كل "ب" إما أن يكون "د" وإما "أ") لضرورة امتناع الخلو من "ج" و "د" وأن "أ" لا يخلو عن كونه خاصاً أو عاماً للجزأين، وعلى كلا التقديرين فصدق المنع من الخلو منهما، ولا يلزم مثله في المنع والجمع ؛ لجواز أن يكون ما ثبت لأحد المتعاندين ثابتاً للآخر وهو أعم منهما.
The first combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always every B is either C or D' and 'Every D is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction preventing void without combination, which is 'Always every B does not lack being either D or A', due to the necessity of forbidding void from C and D, and that A does not lack being specific or general to the two parts, and on both suppositions, the truth of the prevention of void from them, and a similar case does not follow in prevention and combination, because it is possible that what is established for one of the two contraries is established for the other, and it is more general than them.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage describes the first valid combination (iqtiran) of a mixed disjunctive-categorical syllogism. The major premise is a disjunctive with two universal affirmative parts: 'Always every B is either C or D'. The minor is a universal affirmative categorical: 'Every D is A'. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction: 'Always every B is not devoid of being either D or A'. This conclusion prevents void (the two disjuncts cannot both be false) but does not prevent combination (they can both be true). The reasoning: If every B is either C or D, then excluding both is impossible. Since A is either specific (akhas) or general (a'am) to C and D, it follows that no B can lack A entirely—every B is either D or A. The author notes that the prevention of combination (man' al-jam') does not necessarily hold because what is true for one contrary may also be true for the other if it is more general.

Key Terms
الاقتران الأول
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
المتعاندان

D) Some B is not A.

Key Takeaway
The first iqtiran demonstrates that from a universal affirmative disjunction (preventing void) and a universal affirmative categorical, the conclusion is a disjunction of the same type that prevents void but not combination.

Second Iqtiran: Universal Affirmative Disjunction with Particular Negative Parts
Author presents the second combination of disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the second iqtiran and analyze its reasoning, recognizing potential logical issues.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative disjunctions can have particular negative parts, but the inference to a universal conclusion requires scrutiny.

In this second combination, the disjunctive premise is a universal affirmative statement whose parts are particular negatives. The author combines it with a universal affirmative minor and claims a conclusion similar to the first iqtiran. We will break down this structure and examine the logical steps, noting where the reasoning may rely on hidden assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the universal affirmative disjunction: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D.' Note that its parts are particular negatives: 'not every B is C' and 'not every B is D'.
The word 'always' indicates the disjunction holds universally.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is a universal affirmative: 'Every D is A.' This is a categorical proposition connecting D to A.
The middle term here is D.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the first disjunct
The text says: if 'not every B is C' is true, then 'every B is D' is true. Ask yourself: does that logically follow from the disjunction alone? Consider the relationship between C and D.
This step seems to assume an exclusive relation not stated in the premises.
4
Analyze the reasoning for the second disjunct
Similarly, if 'not every B is D' is true, then 'every B is C' is true. Again evaluate the logical necessity.
The OCR note suggests a possible error: 'ليس كل "د" "ب"' might be 'ليس كل "ب" "د"'.
5
Compare with the first iqtiran
The conclusion is said to be like the first iqtiran. What might that conclusion be? Likely a universal affirmative disjunction involving A and B. Consider how the minor premise is used.
Without the first iqtiran passage, we infer the conclusion is a disjunction about B and A.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات سالبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "ب" "د") و(كل "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "ب" "ج") صدق ( كل "ب" "د") وكان (كل "د" "أ") وإن صدق ليس كل "د" "ب") صدق (كل "ب" "ج").
The second combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D' and 'Every D is A' and its conclusion is like the preceding one; because if 'not every B is C' is true, then 'every B is D' is true, and then 'every D is A' so [conclusion follows]; and if 'not every D is B' is true, then 'every B is C' is true.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage describes the second combination. The major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction but its parts are particular negatives: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D'. The minor premise is 'Every D is A'. The conclusion is the same type as the first combination: a universal affirmative disjunction that prevents void. The reasoning: If 'not every B is C' is true, then by the relation between the disjuncts (which are contradictory in a way), 'every B is D' becomes true. Combined with 'every D is A', we get that every B is A. If 'not every B is D' is true, then 'every B is C' is true, but since we need a connection to A, the argument seems to rely on the fact that A is common. Actually, the text says: 'if not every B is C is true, then every B is D is true'—this follows from the disjunction being exclusive? Possibly a misreading. The key is that the conclusion is the same as iqtiran 1.

كليتين موجبتين (kulliyyatayn mūjibatayn) - two universal affirmatives

Third Iqtiran: Universal Affirmative Disjuncts with Particular Affirmative Parts
Author presents the third combination: universal affirmative disjuncts with particular affirmative parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the third combination of disjunctive syllogism with particular affirmative parts and derive the correct conclusion.

Core Idea
In this compound syllogism, the disjunction's parts are particular, and the categorical premise links one disjunct to a new term.

This lesson examines a specific compound syllogism where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular affirmative parts, and the minor is a universal affirmative categorical. The conclusion is a new disjunction. Understanding this structure is crucial for advanced syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic, as it appears in complex arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
The major premise is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D'. Note that the parts are particular affirmative (juz'iyyat mujiba).
2
Identify the categorical premise
The minor premise is 'Every D is A', a universal affirmative categorical proposition.
3
Understand the logical relationship
The disjunction ensures that at least one part is true (mana'at al-khuluw). The categorical relates D to A.
The text uses 'always' indicating the disjunction is universal in time.
4
Derive the conclusion
If 'some B is C' is true, we cannot directly get 'some B is A' from the minor. However, if 'some B is D' is true, then from 'every D is A' we get 'some B is A'. The disjunction ensures that if one part is false, the other is true. So the conclusion is a disjunction of the two possibilities: 'Either some B is C or some B is A'.
The text's reasoning may be compressed; the key is that the conclusion follows from the structure.
5
Verify validity
Check if the conclusion necessarily follows. In this form, it does: given the premises, the conclusion holds for all cases. This is a valid compound syllogism.
Note the potential flaw in the source text's explanation; the formal validity stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث من موجبتين كليتين وأجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "ب" "د") و(كل "د" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأجزاء الانفصال فيها جزئية موجبة، وهي إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "ب" "أ") لأنه إن صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "د" "أ") فـ (بعض "ب" "أ") وإن كذب (بعض "ب" "د") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "ج").
The third combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as in our saying: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D' and 'Every D is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction preventing void without combination, and the parts of the disjunction in it are particular affirmative, namely: either some B is C or some B is A, because if 'some B is C' is true and 'every D is A', then 'some B is A' [follows]; and if 'some B is D' is false, then 'some B is C' is true.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage covers the third combination. The major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular affirmative parts: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D'. The minor is 'Every D is A'. The conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction (preventing void) whose parts are particular affirmative: 'Either some B is C or some B is A'. The reasoning: If 'some B is C' is true, then from the minor 'every D is A' we cannot directly get 'some B is A' because C is not connected to A; but the text says 'if some B is C is true and every D is A, then some B is A' — this seems to require that C implies D? Actually, the disjunction ensures that if one part is false, the other is true. So if 'some B is C' is true, we don't know about D. The reasoning might be: if 'some B is C' is false, then 'some B is D' is true, and then 'some B is A' follows. But the text says the opposite. Possibly a misphrasing. Anyway, the result is a disjunction with parts 'some B is C' and 'some B is A'.

Fourth Combination: Universal Negative Disjuncts
Fourth iqtiran: universal affirmative disjuncts with universal negative parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth iqtiran form and validate its conclusion using exclusive disjunction reasoning.

Core Idea
Universal negative disjuncts with a universal affirmative minor yield a particular affirmative exclusive conclusion.

This lesson unpacks the fourth compound syllogism (iqtiran) in the Daqaiq al-Haqaiq tradition. Here the major premise is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction whose parts are universal negatives, and the minor is a universal affirmative. The reasoning forces a particular affirmative exclusive conclusion. Understanding this form sharpens your ability to evaluate compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise type
The major is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction (manniat al-jam' wal-khuluw) with two universal negative parts: 'Always either no B is C or no B is D'.
The phrase 'always' indicates universality; the parts are 'no B is C' and 'no B is D'.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor is a universal affirmative: 'Every D is A'. The term D appears in the second disjunct.
The minor links D to A.
3
Reason from the first disjunct
Assume 'no B is C' is true. Because the disjunction is exclusive, the other part 'no B is D' must be false. Therefore its contradictory 'some B is D' is true. Then from 'every D is A' we derive 'some B is A'.
Exclusivity ensures that one true disjunct forces the other false.
4
Reason from the second disjunct
Similarly, assume 'no B is D' is true; then 'no B is C' is false, so 'some B is C' is true.
The conclusion disjuncts are 'some B is C' and 'some B is A'.
5
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction with particular parts: 'Always either some B is C or some B is A' (exclusive).
Like the previous iqtiran, the conclusion's parts are particular affirmatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الرابع من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات سوالب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د") و(كل "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق لا شيء من "ب" "ج") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "د") وكان (كل) "د" "أ") فـ (بعض "ب" "أ") وإن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "د") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "ج").
The fourth combination: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as in our saying: 'Always either no B is C or no B is D' and 'Every D is A', and its conclusion is like the one before it; because if 'no B is C' is true, then 'some B is D' is true, and 'every D is A', so 'some B is A'; and if 'no B is D' is true, then 'some B is C' is true.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth combination. The major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction with universal negative parts: 'Always either no B is C or no B is D'. The minor is 'Every D is A'. The conclusion is the same as the previous one: a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction with particular parts, specifically 'either some B is C or some B is A' (implied by the reasoning). The reasoning: If 'no B is C' is true, then the disjunction forces that 'no B is D' is false, so its negation 'some B is D' is true. Then from 'every D is A', we get 'some B is A'. Similarly, if 'no B is D' is true, then 'some B is C' is true. Thus the conclusion disjuncts are 'some B is C' and 'some B is A'.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
The fourth iqtiran shows that from an exclusive universal negative disjunction and a universal affirmative minor, a particular affirmative exclusive conclusion follows.

Fifth Iqtiran: Mixed Universal Affirmative Disjunctive and Universal Negative Categorical Syllogism
Author presents the fifth combination: disjuncts universal affirmative and negative, categorical universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a mixed universal affirmative disjunctive and universal negative categorical syllogism (fifth iqtiran) and verify its validity using the two-case reasoning.

Core Idea
A universal negative categorical premise flips the quality of one conclusion disjunct.

The fifth iqtiran introduces a new pattern where the categorical premise is universal negative, unlike the previous four. This produces a conclusion disjunction with parts of different quality—one affirmative, one negative. The logical derivation relies on two cases: if one disjunct is true, the categorical premise yields a negative proposition; if it is false, the other disjunct is affirmed. Mastering this combination strengthens your ability to handle complex syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find the universal affirmative exclusive disjunction. In the passage, it is 'Always either every B is C or every B is D'. The two parts are both universal affirmative: 'every B is C' and 'every B is D'.
Exclusive disjunction means exactly one part is true.
2
Identify the categorical premise
Locate the universal negative categorical premise. Here it is 'No D is A'. This premise will interact with one of the disjuncts to produce the conclusion.
3
Analyze the two-case derivation
Consider two cases based on the disjunct 'every B is D'. If it is true, then from 'No D is A' and 'every B is D', we derive 'No B is A' via a first-figure syllogism (Celarent). If it is false, the exclusive disjunction forces 'every B is C' to be true.
4
Construct the conclusion disjunction
Combine the two outcomes: one case gives 'every B is C', the other gives 'No B is A'. Since exactly one holds, the conclusion is the exclusive disjunction 'Always either every B is C or no B is A'. Note the quality difference: one affirmative, one negative.
The conclusion is universal affirmative disjunctive with parts differing in quality (mukhtalifat al-kayfiyya).
5
Verify the logical flow
Check that the derivation is valid: the categorical premise 'No D is A' with 'every B is D' yields 'No B is A' (first-figure, valid). The exclusive disjunction ensures the other case is covered. Thus the conclusion follows necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة مختلفة الكيفية، وهي دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ") لأنه إن صدق كل "ب" "د") وكان (لا شيء من "د" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإن كذب (كل "ب" "د") فقد صدق (كل "ب" "ج").
The fifth combination: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal affirmative and universal negative categorical, as in our saying: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D' and 'No D is A', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction whose parts are universal and differ in quality, namely: 'Always either every B is C or no B is A', because if 'every B is D' is true and 'no D is A', then 'no B is A'; and if 'every B is D' is false, then 'every B is C' is true.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage covers the fifth combination. Here the categorical premise is universal negative ('No D is A'), unlike the previous four where it was universal affirmative. The disjunctive premise is universal affirmative with two universal affirmative parts: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D'. The conclusion is a universal exclusive disjunction with parts of different quality: 'Always either every B is C or no B is A'. The reasoning: If 'every B is D' is true, then from 'no D is A' we get 'no B is A' (by simple conversion? Actually, from 'no D is A' we cannot directly get 'no B is A' unless every B is D, which is given). So the conclusion's second part is 'no B is A'. If 'every B is D' is false, then by the disjunction 'every B is C' is true. Thus the conclusion disjuncts are 'every B is C' and 'no B is A'.

اقتران (iqtiran)
منفصلة (munfasila)

Key Takeaway
In the fifth iqtiran, a universal negative categorical premise combined with a universal affirmative disjunction yields a conclusion disjunction with parts differing in quality—one affirmative, one negative.

The Sixth Iqtiran: Disjunctive with Particular Negative Parts
Author presents sixth combination: particular negative disjuncts with universal negative categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the sixth iqtiran by identifying its disjunctive and categorical premises and deriving its conclusion following the same pattern as the fifth iqtiran.

Core Idea
Particular negative disjuncts yield universal conclusions through complementary universal premises.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth combination, where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction whose two parts are particular negative propositions, and the minor is a universal negative categorical. The author shows that the conclusion mirrors the fifth iqtiran, but with mixed-quality disjuncts. Understanding this requires careful handling of the logical equivalences between particular and universal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the disjunctive major premise: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D' (universal affirmative disjunction with two particular negatives). Then the categorical minor: 'No D is A' (universal negative).
The disjunctive parts are particular negatives, not universals.
2
Consider the case when the first disjunct is true
Assume 'not every B is C' is true. Then the second disjunct 'not every B is D' must be false (because the disjunction is exclusive? Actually, it's a universal affirmative disjunction, meaning always one of them holds, but not both? In context, it seems the disjunction is such that if one is true, the other is false. So the negation of the false disjunct is true: 'every B is D' is true.
Converting 'not every B is D' false to 'every B is D' true is a standard logical equivalence.
3
Apply the categorical minor
Now we have 'every B is D' and 'no D is A'. This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara? Actually, 'every B is D' and 'no D is A' gives 'no B is A' by Celarent? Check: All B are D, No D is A, therefore No B is A. That's valid, first figure, mood Celarent.
This step uses the universal affirmative derived from the disjunction.
4
Consider the symmetric case
If instead 'not every B is D' is true, then 'every B is C' is true. But there is no categorical premise with C and A directly. The text says the conclusion is 'like the one before it', so the conclusion will be a disjunction: either 'every B is C' or 'no B is A'. However, in this symmetric case, we do not derive a new conclusion from the second assumption alone; the conclusion must be a disjunction covering both possibilities.
The final conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction with mixed parts: 'either every B is C or no B is A'.

Commentary
This passage describes the sixth combination. The major premise is a universal affirmative disjunction with particular negative parts: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D'. The minor is universal negative: 'No D is A'. The conclusion is the same as the fifth: a universal affirmative exclusive disjunction with mixed quality parts: 'Either not every B is C? Actually, the conclusion is 'like the one before it', so it should be 'either every B is C or no B is A'? But careful: The reasoning: if 'not every B is C' is true, then by the disjunction, 'not every B is D' is false, so its negation 'every B is D' is true. Then from 'no D is A', we get 'no B is A'. If 'not every B is D' is true, then 'every B is C' is true. So the conclusion disjuncts seem to be 'every B is C' and 'no B is A'. But the disjunction in the conclusion is universal and mixed. So it matches the fifth.

Four Compound Syllogisms with Universal Affirmative Disjunctive Major
Author lists four compound syllogisms with disjunctive major and categorical minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion of any compound syllogism with a universal affirmative disjunctive major and a universal negative categorical minor, and verify the reasoning using truth conditions.

Core Idea
In compound syllogisms, the exclusivity of the disjunctive yields a new disjunctive conclusion.

In this passage, the author systematically lists four specific forms of compound syllogisms. Each consists of a universal affirmative disjunctive major premise and a universal negative categorical minor premise. The conclusion is always a universal affirmative disjunctive with parts of differing quality. Understanding these forms equips you with a powerful tool for reasoning with exclusive alternatives in Islamic law and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
Find the universal affirmative disjunctive (دائماً إما ... وإما ...). Note the two parts, each a categorical proposition with the same subject.
The disjunctive is assumed exclusive (مانعة الجمع).
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor is always a universal negative categorical (لا شيء من ... ...). Its subject matches one of the disjuncts' predicates.
The predicate of the minor becomes part of the conclusion.
3
Determine the conclusion pattern
The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive where one part is the first part of the major and the other is a universal negative with the minor's predicate applied to the common subject.
For the fifth conjunction, conclusion: 'always either every B is J or nothing of B is A'.
4
Verify using truth conditions
If the first disjunct is true, the second is false; if the second is false, the first is true. Combine with the categorical to derive the disjunctive conclusion.
This mirrors modus tollendo ponens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة موجبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كليّة مختلفة الكيفية، وهي دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ") لأنه إن صدق كل "ب" "د") وكان (لا شيء من "د" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإن كذب (كل "ب" "د") فقد صدق (كل "ب" "ج"). الاقتران السادس من كلية موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها جزئية سالبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "ب" "ج") صدق كل "ب" "د") وكان لا شيء من "د" "أ") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وإن صدق ليس كل "ب" "د") صدق (كل "ب" "ج"). الاقتران السابع من كلية موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها جزئية موجبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما بعض "ب" "ج" وإما بعض "ب" "د") و (لا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته (دائمـاً إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "ب" "ج") لأنه إن صدق (بعض "ب" "د") وكان لا شيء من "د" "أ") فـ (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإن كذب (بعض "ب" "د") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "ج"). الاقتران الثامن من كلية موجبة منفصلة أجزاؤها كلية سالبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: (دائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "د") و(لا شيء من "د" "أ") ونتيجته (دائماً إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "ب" "ج") لأنه إن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "د") وكان (لا شيء من "د" "أ") ف (بعض "ب" ليس "أ") وإن صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "د") فقد صدق (بعض "ب" "ج"). وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة قد يتركب منها ومن الحَمْلية اقترانات أُخَر ثمانية موازية لهذه الاقترانات، إلا أن النتيجة فيها تكون جزئية موجبة منفصلة.
The fifth conjunction is from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a universal affirmative and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: (always either every "B" is "J" or every "B" is "D") and (nothing of "D" is "A"), and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal differing in quality, which is always either every "B" is "J" or nothing of "B" is "A") because if every "B" is "D" is true and (nothing of "D" is "A") then (nothing of "B" is "A"), and if (every "B" is "D") is false then (every "B" is "J") is true. The sixth conjunction is from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a particular negative and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: (always either not every "B" is "J" or not every "B" is "D") and (nothing of "D" is "A"), and its conclusion is like the previous because if not every "B" is "J" is true then every "B" is "D" is true and nothing of "D" is "A" then (nothing of "B" is "A"), and if not every "B" is "D" is true then (every "B" is "J") is true. The seventh conjunction is from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a particular affirmative and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: (always either some "B" is "J" or some "B" is "D") and (nothing of "D" is "A"), and its conclusion is (always either some "B" is not "A" or some "B" is "J") because if some "B" is "D" is true and nothing of "D" is "A" then some "B" is not "A", and if some "B" is "D" is false then some "B" is "J" is true. The eighth conjunction is from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a universal negative and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: (always either nothing of "B" is "J" or nothing of "B" is "D") and (nothing of "D" is "A"), and its conclusion is (always either some "B" is not "A" or some "B" is "J") because if nothing of "B" is "J" is true then some "B" is "D" is true and nothing of "D" is "A" then some "B" is not "A", and if nothing of "B" is "D" is true then some "B" is "J" is true. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, there may be composed from it and the categorical other eight conjunctions parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage enumerates four specific forms of compound syllogisms (اقترانات) where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunctive proposition and the minor premise is a universal negative categorical proposition. Each entry (fifth to eighth) shows a different combination of the quantity and quality of the two parts of the disjunctive. The author provides the conclusion form and a justification using the truth conditions of the disjunctive. For example, in the fifth, if the disjunctive 'always either every B is J or every B is D' is true, and we know 'nothing of D is A', then if every B is D, we get nothing of B is A; if not, then every B is J. The passage then notes that if the disjunctive is particular affirmative instead of universal affirmative, there are eight parallel forms, but the conclusion becomes a particular affirmative disjunctive. This careful enumeration is typical of logical manuals that exhaustively list valid syllogistic forms. For an Islamic science illustration: Suppose a disjunctive 'Every act of worship is either obligatory or supererogatory' and a categorical 'No supererogatory act removes a duty' might yield conclusions about obligation.

Key Terms
اقتران
منفصلة
حملیة
نتيجة

Checkpoint

A) Nothing of B is A
B) Every B is J
C) Some B is not A
D) Every B is not J

Key Takeaway
In compound syllogisms with a universal affirmative disjunctive major and a universal negative categorical minor, the conclusion is always a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are derived from the disjunctive parts and the categorical predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Check the validity: If you have 'Always either every sale is permissible or every sale is prohibited' and 'No prohibited sale produces profit', what conclusion follows? Use the truth condition method.

Parallel Forms with Particular Affirmative Disjunctive Major
Author notes eight parallel forms when disjunctive is particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight parallel forms when the disjunctive major is particular affirmative and predict that the conclusion becomes a particular affirmative disjunctive.

Core Idea
Changing the quantity of the disjunctive major from universal to particular alters the conclusion's quantity.

The passage notes that if the disjunctive major is particular affirmative instead of universal affirmative, there are eight parallel forms, but the conclusion becomes a particular affirmative disjunctive. This reveals the sensitivity of compound syllogisms to the quantity of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the change in major premise
Instead of universal affirmative disjunctive, now it is particular affirmative (e.g., دائماً إما بعض ب ج أو بعض ب د). The minor remains universal negative categorical.
The form is parallel in structure but with particular quantifiers.
2
Identify the eight parallel forms
The author states there are eight such forms, likely corresponding to each of the four original forms having two variants (since the parts can be particular affirmative or particular negative, etc.).
The exact list is not given, but the pattern is that the conclusion is particular affirmative disjunctive.
3
Determine the conclusion pattern
The conclusion will be a particular affirmative disjunctive, e.g., 'always either some B is not A or some B is J' or similar forms.
The conclusion's parts are derived similarly but now are particular.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Construct an example from fiqh: 'Some acts of worship are either obligatory or supererogatory' and 'No supererogatory act removes a duty' yields a particular conclusion.
This helps internalize the logical form.

Commentary
This passage enumerates four specific forms of compound syllogisms (اقترانات) where the major premise is a universal affirmative disjunctive proposition and the minor premise is a universal negative categorical proposition. Each entry (fifth to eighth) shows a different combination of the quantity and quality of the two parts of the disjunctive. The author provides the conclusion form and a justification using the truth conditions of the disjunctive. For example, in the fifth, if the disjunctive 'always either every B is J or every B is D' is true, and we know 'nothing of D is A', then if every B is D, we get nothing of B is A; if not, then every B is J. The passage then notes that if the disjunctive is particular affirmative instead of universal affirmative, there are eight parallel forms, but the conclusion becomes a particular affirmative disjunctive. This careful enumeration is typical of logical manuals that exhaustively list valid syllogistic forms. Second Figure Compound Syllogisms: First Four Valid Forms
Author defines second figure conditions and lists four valid compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a second figure compound syllogism and construct the conclusion for any of the first four valid conjunctions.

Core Idea
The second figure links disjunctive major and categorical minor through a common middle term as predicate.

In this lesson, we explore the second figure of compound syllogisms, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The author assumes the disjunctive parts agree in quality and quantity, and the categorical minor must be universal. We will walk through the first four valid conjunctions and their conclusions, using the author's own proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common middle term
Find the term that appears as a predicate in both the disjunctive major and the categorical minor. In the notation, this is 'ب' (B).
2
Verify the conditions
Check that the disjunctive major is affirmative and its parts agree in quality and quantity (e.g., both universal affirmatives). Also ensure the categorical minor is universal.
The author states: 'شرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وكليّة الحَمْلية'.
3
Identify the pattern of the parts
For each of the first four conjunctions, note whether the disjunctive parts are universal affirmative, particular negative, particular affirmative, or universal negative.
The text lists them in order: first: كليات موجبة, second: جزئیات سوالب, third: جزئيات موجبة, fourth: كليات سوالب.
4
Apply the truth condition to derive the conclusion
Use the author's reasoning: if one part of the disjunction is true, the other is false; combine with the categorical premise to deduce the disjunctive conclusion.
For example, in the first: from 'every B is J' and 'every A is D', we get 'not every B is A'.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Replace the variables with Islamic legal terms (e.g., prayer, obligation, permissibility) and verify the conclusion follows the same logical form.
This reinforces the abstract pattern with concrete Islamic reasoning.

Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author enumerates productive conjunctions for second figure disjunctive syllogisms and conditions for third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the eight productive conjunctions of a second-figure disjunctive-categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Each conjunction of disjunctive major and categorical minor yields a unique valid conclusion.

The author now enumerates all valid conjunctions for disjunctive-categorical syllogisms in the second figure, where the common middle term is the predicate of both premises. This systematic enumeration shows the logical structure behind compound disjunctive reasoning, a key tool in Islamic jurisprudence and theology. The conditions for production are that the disjunctive must be affirmative and the categorical must be universal. The passage provides examples with placeholder terms and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and conditions
The second figure requires the middle term to be the predicate in both premises. The author states the condition for production: the disjunctive must be affirmative and the categorical universal. Confirm this in the passage.
The middle term is 'B' in the examples.
2
Extract the quality and quantity of premises
For each conjunction, note the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the disjunctive parts and the categorical minor. The disjunctive major is always universal affirmative, but its parts vary.
The author treats four combinations for the categorical being universal affirmative (conjunctions 1-4) and four for it being universal negative (5-8).
3
Derive the conclusion via case analysis
The proof considers each disjunct's truth. For example, in the first conjunction, if 'Every B is C' is true, then the other disjunct 'Every B is D' must be false (since exclusive disjunction is implied), leading via the categorical premise to 'Not every B is A'.
The conclusion is always a disjunction of particular propositions.
4
Verify the proof's validity
Check that the reasoning is sound: the categorical premise negates one part of the disjunction, forcing the other part to be true. The conclusion follows the pattern of modus tollendo ponens applied to quantified propositions.
The author provides explicit proofs for each conjunction.

يكون الحد الأوسط المشترك موضوعاً فيهما، ولنفرض أيضاً الكلام في ما إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال متفقة الكيفية والكمية، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون الحملية كلية إن كانت أجزاء الانفصال كليات سالبة، Eight [conjunctions] parallel to these conjunctions, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative disjunctive. As for if it [the disjunctive] is in the pattern of the second figure, which is that the common middle term is predicated in both [premises], and we suppose the discussion when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity, and the condition for its production is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and the universality of the categorical – and its productive conjunctions are sixteen conjunctions. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, as we say: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D' and 'Every A is D', and its conclusion: 'Always either some B is not C or some B is not A', because if 'Every B is C' is true, then 'Not every B is D' is true, and 'Every A is D', so 'Not every B is A'; and if 'Every B is D' is true, then 'Not every B is C'. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as we say: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D' and 'Every A is D', and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'Not every B is D' is true, and 'Every A is D', then 'Not every B is A'; and if 'Not every B is D' is false, then 'Not every B is C' is true. The third conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as we say: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D' and 'Every A is D', and its conclusion: 'Either no B is A or no B is C', because if 'Some B is C' is true, then 'No B is D' is true, and 'Every A is D', so 'No B is A'; and if 'Some B is D' is true, then 'No B is C' is true. The fourth conjunction: from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as we say: 'Always either no B is C or no B is D' and 'Every A is D', and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'No B is D' is true, and 'Every A is D', so 'No B is A'; and if 'No B is D' is false, then 'No B is C' is true. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a universal affirmative and a universal negative categorical, as we say: 'Always either every B is C or every B is D' and 'No A is D', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are universal different, namely 'Always either no B is A or every B is C', because if 'Every B is D' is true and 'No A is D', then 'No B is A'; and if 'Every B is D' is false, then 'Every B is C' is true. The sixth conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive and its parts are a particular negative and a universal negative categorical, as we say: 'Always either not every B is C or not every B is D' and 'No A is D', and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'Not every B is C' is true, then 'Every B is D' is true, and 'No A is D', so 'No B is A'; and if 'Not every B is D' is true, then 'Every B is C' is true. The seventh conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive and its parts are a particular affirmative and a universal negative categorical, as we say: 'Always either some B is C or some B is D' and 'No A is D', and its conclusion: 'Always either some B is not A or some B is C', because if 'Some B is D' is true and 'No A is D', then 'Some B is not A'; and if 'Some B is D' is false, then 'Some B is C' is true. The eighth conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive and its parts are universal negatives and a universal negative categorical, as we say: 'Always either no B is C or no B is D' and 'No A is D', and its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'No B is D' is true, then 'Some B is C' is true? [sic] and if 'No B is C' is true, then 'Some B is D' is true, and 'No A is D', so 'Some B is not A'. And if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, then eight other conjunctions can be formed from it and the categorical on the pattern of these conjunctions, so you must consider them. And if it [the disjunctive] is in the pattern of the third figure, which is that the common middle term is the subject in both [premises], and we also suppose the discussion when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity, and the condition for its production is the affirmation of the disjunctive, and that the categorical be universal if the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, or particular affirmatives, and its productive conjunctions are twenty-four conjunctions:
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas munfasil) within the framework of categorical (iqtirani) syllogisms. The author has previously discussed conjunctions where the disjunction is particular affirmative; here he explains eight additional conjunctions when the disjunction is universal affirmative and the categorical premise is particular or universal. The passage is primarily enumerative and technical. The author first addresses the second figure, where the common middle term is the predicate in both premises. The condition for production is that the disjunctive be affirmative and the categorical universal. There are sixteen productive conjunctions in this figure, but the author only enumerates eight, assuming the other eight are analogous. For each conjunction, he provides the form, an example, and the proof. For instance, in the first conjunction, both premises are universal affirmative, and the disjunctive parts are universal affirmative. The example uses placeholder terms A, B, C, D. The conclusion is a particular affirmative disjunctive. The proof proceeds by considering the truth of one part of the disjunction and deriving the conclusion via the categorical premise. The second through fourth conjunctions vary the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts, but the categorical remains universal affirmative. The fifth through eighth conjunctions use a universal negative categorical premise. The author then notes that if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, there are another eight conjunctions on the same pattern, leaving them to the reader. Finally, he moves to the third figure, where the common middle term is the subject in both premises. The condition is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and the categorical be universal if the disjunctive parts are universal negatives, or particular affirmatives. The productive conjunctions are stated to be twenty-four, but the passage ends without listing them. The student should grasp the systematic nature of these conjunctions: each combination of quality and quantity of premises yields a specific conclusion, derived through the relationship of the middle term.

First Iqtiran Pattern: Universal Affirmative Disjuncts and Categorical Premise
Author defines the first conjunctive pattern with universal affirmative disjuncts and categorical premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first iqtiran pattern (universal affirmative disjunction + universal affirmative categorical) and determine that its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction that prevents emptiness but not combination, with particular parts.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative disjunction plus a universal affirmative categorical yield a conclusion that bars both false but allows both true.

In this lesson, we explore the first pattern of compound syllogisms that combine a disjunctive premise (munfasila) with a categorical premise (hamliyya). The author presents a specific case where both the disjuncts and the categorical premise are universal affirmative. Understanding this pattern helps you see how logical combinations produce new disjunctions with precise modal properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the two premises: the first is a universal affirmative disjunction (e.g., 'always either every B is C or every B is D'), the second is a universal affirmative categorical (e.g., 'every B is A').
The disjunction uses 'daiman' (always) to indicate universality.
2
Extract the middle term
The term 'B' appears in both premises and serves as the common subject. It links the two premises.
Ensure the categorical premise's subject matches the subject of the disjuncts.
3
Analyze the conclusion form
The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction but its parts are particular: 'always either some C is A or some D is A'. It is 'maniat al-khuluw dun al-jam' (prevents emptiness but not combination).
Check why: if first disjunct true, then some C is A; if second, then some D is A. They can both be true.
4
Construct your own example
Using an Islamic science example, build a valid first iqtiran pattern. For instance, in fiqh: 'Either every sale is permissible or every sale is prohibited' (disjunction) and 'every sale is a transaction' (categorical).
The conclusion must be a disjunction with particular parts: 'either some transactions are permissible or some transactions are prohibited'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و(كل "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأجزاؤها جزئية، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وإما بعض "د" "أ".
The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, as we say: (always either every "B" is "C" or [every "B" is] "D") and (every "B" is "A"), and its result is a universal affirmative disjunction that prevents emptiness but not combination, and its parts are particular, and it is always either some "C" is "A" or some "D" is "A".
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines the first pattern (iqtiran) of a conjunctive syllogism that combines a disjunctive premise (munfasila) and a categorical premise (hamliyya). The disjunctive premise has two universal affirmative disjuncts: 'always either every B is C or every B is D'. The categorical premise is also universal affirmative: 'every B is A'. The middle term 'B' is common. The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction: 'always either some C is A or some D is A'. The author notes that the conclusion is 'maniat al-khuluww dun al-jam' — i.e., it prevents emptiness (the two parts cannot both be false) but does not prevent combination (they can both be true). The parts of the conclusion are particular (some C is A, some D is A). The reasoning: if the first disjunct is true (every B is C), then since every B is A, it follows that some C is A. If the second disjunct is true (every B is D), then similarly some D is A. Hence the disjunctive conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The first iqtiran pattern combines a universal affirmative disjunction with a universal affirmative categorical to produce a universal affirmative disjunction that bars both-false but allows both-true, with particular parts.

Conditions for the Fourth Figure Mixed Syllogism
Author states conditions for the fourth figure of mixed syllogisms and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the fourth figure and determine whether a given combination satisfies them.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's validity hinges on the quantity and quality of both premises.

We have already learned about disjunctive syllogisms in earlier figures. Now the author introduces the fourth figure, where the middle term's position is reversed: it becomes the subject of the disjuncts and the predicate in the categorical. This reversal introduces new conditions that must be carefully applied to ensure a sound conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure definition
The passage defines the fourth figure: the middle term (الحد الأوسط) is the subject of the disjuncts in the disjunctive premise and the predicate in the categorical premise. Note this reversal from earlier figures.
2
Extract the conditions
List the explicit conditions: (1) the disjunction must be affirmative; (2) the categorical must not be particular negative; (3) if the disjuncts are particular affirmative or universal negative, the categorical must not be particular; (4) if the categorical is particular affirmative, the disjuncts must be universal affirmative or particular negative.
3
Examine the example
Take the first productive combination: two universal affirmative disjuncts and a universal affirmative categorical. Write out the example: 'دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل ب د' and 'كل أ ب'. Verify that it meets all conditions.
4
Derive the conclusion
From the premises, since every A is B, and B is either entirely C or entirely D, it follows that every A is either C or D. The text states the result is a universal affirmative, likely meaning a universal disjunctive proposition.
5
Note the significance
The author mentions twenty productive conjuncts (qara'in). Only the first is given, but understanding this pattern allows you to recognize valid mixed syllogisms in the fourth figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان على نمط الشكل الرابع وهو أن يكون الحد الأوسط موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال ومحمولاً في الحَمْلية، ولنفرض الكلام إذا كانت أجزاء الانفصال متفقة الكيفية والكمية وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن لا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال جزئيات موجبة أو كليات سوالب وأن لا تكون الحَمْلية جزئية وإن كانت الحَمْلية جزئية موجبة فلا بُدّ وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سوالب وقرائنه المنتجة عشرون اقتراناً : الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا: (دائماً إمـا كـل "ب" "ج" وإمـا كـل "ب" "د") و(كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كليـة موجبة.
And as for if it is on the pattern of the fourth figure, which is that the middle term is the subject for the parts of the disjunction and the predicate in the categorical, and let us assume the discussion when the parts of the disjunction agree in quality and quantity, and its condition for productivity is the affirmativeness of the disjunction, and that the categorical not be particular negative, and if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, then the categorical must not be particular, and if the categorical is particular affirmative then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjuncts (qara'in) are twenty: The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, as we say: (always either every "B" is "C" or every "B" is "D") and (every "A" is "B"), and its result is a universal [affirmative?].
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the fourth figure, where the middle term is the subject of the disjuncts in the disjunctive premise and the predicate in the categorical premise. The author first sets the scenario: the disjuncts agree in quality and quantity (i.e., are all affirmative or all negative, and all universal or all particular). The key condition for productivity is that the disjunctive premise must be affirmative. Additionally, the categorical premise must not be particular negative. If the disjuncts are particular affirmative or universal negative, then the categorical must not be particular at all. If the categorical is particular affirmative, then the disjuncts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. With these conditions met, there are twenty productive 'qara'in' (combinations of quantity and quality). The author then gives the first of these: a disjunctive premise with two universal affirmative disjuncts and a universal affirmative categorical. The example is: 'always either every B is C or every B is D' and 'every A is B'. Note the reversal of the categorical: here the middle term B is predicate in the categorical premise (كل أ ب), whereas in earlier patterns it was subject. This is characteristic of the fourth figure.

Valid Conjunctions in the Fourth Figure: Conditions and Overview
Author enumerates valid conjunctions of disjunctive and categorical propositions in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four groups of valid conjunctions for fourth-figure compound syllogisms and identify the conditions applying to each group.

Core Idea
The fourth figure in compound syllogisms demands specific restrictions on the disjunctive and categorical premises to be productive.

Daqaiq al-Haqaiq presents the rules for combining a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) with a categorical premise (حملیة) in the fourth figure of compound syllogisms. The middle term is subject of the disjuncts and predicate of the categorical. The text enumerates six valid conjunctions out of twenty possible, each with strict conditions on the quantity and quality of the disjuncts and the categorical premise. This lesson surveys the overall framework and the conditions that define the groups of conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Define the fourth figure in compound syllogisms
Note that the fourth figure here is not the standard categorical fourth figure. The middle term is subject in the disjunctive parts and predicate in the categorical premise. Understand this structural difference.
This is essential to avoid confusion with categorical syllogism figures.
2
Extract the general validity conditions
The text states that the disjunctive must be affirmative. The categorical cannot be particular negative. When the disjuncts are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, the categorical cannot be particular. When the categorical is particular affirmative, the disjuncts must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. These conditions restrict the combinations.
Memorize the two main condition groups based on the categorical premise's quality and quantity.
3
Categorize the six conjunctions into groups
The first four conjunctions have a universal affirmative categorical premise; the fifth and sixth have a universal negative categorical premise. Within each group, the disjuncts vary in quality and quantity.
This grouping helps in remembering the forms.
4
Apply the conditions to identify productive combinations
Given a proposed compound syllogism with a disjunctive premise and a categorical premise in the fourth figure, check if the conditions hold: disjunctive must be affirmative; categorical must not be particular negative; and if categorical is particular affirmative, disjuncts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. If categorical is universal, check the conjunction number. Practice with the examples given.
The six enumerated conjunctions are the only productive ones; others are invalid.

الاقتران الثاني: من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء الانفصال جزئیات سوالب كقولنا دائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "ج" وإما ليس كل "ب" "د") و(كل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالذي قبله؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "ب" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" ("د") وإن صدق ليس كل "ب" اد") صدق (كل "ب" "ج"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته كلية موجبة منفصلة مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأجزاؤها كليات سوالب، وهي دائماً إما لا شيء من "ج" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ" لأنه إن صدق (بعض "ب" "ج") صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "د") وكان ( كل "أ" "ب") فـ ( لا شيء من "د" "أ") لما عرف في الحَمْليات، وإن صدق (بعض "ب" "د") صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ب") فـ (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") لما عرف في الحَمْليات، وإن صدق (بعض "ب" "د") صدق (لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان (كل "أ" "ب") فـ (لا شيء من "ج"). الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال كليات سوالب، ونتيجته كالتي قبله. الاقتران الخامس من كليّة موجبة منفصلة وأجزاء الانفصال فيها كلية موجبة وكلية سالبة حَمْلية، كقولنا: دائماً إما أن يكون كل "ب" "ج" وإما "د") و (لا شيء "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة منفصلة، أجزاؤها جزئية سالبة، وهي دائماً إما بعض "ج" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ" لأنه إن صدق (كل "ب") وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس ("أ") وإن صدق كل "ب" "د") وكان لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بعض "د" ليس "أ"). الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله إلا أن أجزاء الانفصال جزئیات سوالب.
As for if it is upon the pattern of the fourth figure, which is that the middle term is the subject of the parts of the disjunction and the predicate in the categorical, and let us assume the discussion when the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quality and quantity; its condition for production is that the disjunctive be affirmative, and that the categorical not be particular negative; and if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives or universal negatives, and that the categorical not be particular; and if the categorical is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Its productive conjunctions are twenty: the first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D', and 'Every A is B', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive exclusive of void without conjunction, and its parts are particular affirmatives, namely 'Always either some J is A or some D is A' – because if 'Every B is J' is true and 'Every A is B' is true, then 'Some J is A'; and if 'Every B is D' is true and 'Every A is B' is true, then 'Some D is A'. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, like our saying: 'Always either not every B is J or not every B is D', and 'Every A is B'; its conclusion is like the previous one; because if 'Not every B is J' is true, then 'Every B is D'; and if 'Not every B is D' is true, then 'Every B is J'. The third conjunction: the conjunction in its condition except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive exclusive of void without conjunction, and its parts are universal negatives, namely 'Always either nothing of J is A or nothing of D is A' – because if 'Some B is J' is true, then 'Nothing of B is D' is true, and 'Every A is B' was true, so 'Nothing of D is A' as known in categoricals; and if 'Some B is D' is true, then 'Nothing of B is J' is true, and 'Every A is B' was true, so 'Nothing of J is A' as known in categoricals; and if 'Some B is D' is true, then 'Nothing of B is J' is true, and 'Every A is B' was true, so 'Nothing of J is A'. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its condition except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, and its conclusion is like the previous one. The fifth conjunction: from a universal affirmative disjunctive whose parts are a universal affirmative and a universal negative categorical, like our saying: 'Always either that every B is J or D', and 'Nothing A is B', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunctive, whose parts are particular negatives, namely 'Always either some J is not A or some D is not A' – because if 'Every B is J' is true and 'Nothing of A is B' is true, then 'Some J is not A'; and if 'Every B is D' is true and 'Nothing of A is B' is true, then 'Some D is not A'. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction in its condition except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for forming valid compound syllogisms (اقترانات) in the fourth figure, where the middle term is subject in the disjunctive parts and predicate in the categorical premise. The author, likely al-Abhari or a commentator, details six productive conjunctions out of a possible twenty. The first conjunction uses universal affirmative disjuncts and a universal affirmative categorical, yielding a universal affirmative disjunctive conclusion of the exclusive type (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) – meaning at least one disjunct is true but both cannot be false together, though both could be true? Actually مانعة الخلو دون الجمع means 'preventing emptiness but not conjunction' i.e., exclusive disjunction where they cannot both be false but may both be true? Typically مانعة الخلو means at least one true, مانعة الجمع means at most one true. مانعة الخلو دون الجمع means 'preventing emptiness (khuluw) but not conjunction (jam')' so it allows both to be true? Standard reading: مانعة الجمع prohibits both true; مانعة الخلو prohibits both false; مانعة الخلو دون الجمع is the same as exclusive disjunction? Actually in logic, exclusive disjunction prohibits both true and both false, but here it says 'دون الجمع' meaning without prohibition of conjunction, so it permits both true but forbids both false. That is important. The text uses the example: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D' with 'Every A is B' giving 'Always either some J is A or some D is A'. The author then explains the reasoning using categorical conversions. The second conjunction uses universal affirmative categorical but the disjuncts are particular negatives ('not every B is J' is an O proposition). The conclusion is same type. Third conjunction uses particular affirmative disjuncts and yields universal negative parts in conclusion. Fourth is similar but with universal negative disjuncts. Fifth conjunction involves a universal affirmative disjunctive with mixed parts (one universal affirmative, one universal negative) and a universal negative categorical premise, yielding particular negative conclusions. Sixth is similar with particular negative disjuncts. All examples are fully formal, using letters (أ, ب, ج, د) as term variables. The passage demonstrates the rigorous enumeration of valid syllogistic forms in the Aristotelian tradition as adopted by Islamic logicians.

In the first conjunction of the fourth figure, what is the conclusion type when both premises are universal affirmative and the disjuncts are universal affirmative?
A) A universal affirmative categorical
B) A universal affirmative disjunctive of the type مانعة الخلو دون الجمع with particular affirmative parts
C) A particular affirmative disjunctive
D) A universal negative disjunctive

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure in compound syllogisms with a disjunctive premise is governed by strict conditions on the quantity and quality of the disjuncts and the categorical premise, yielding six valid conjunctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following disjunctive premise: 'Always, either every revealed law (shar') is obligatory or every revealed law is recommended.' Assume the categorical premise: 'Every act of prayer (salat) is a revealed law.' Using the pattern of the first conjunction, construct the conclusion. Check that the conditions (disjunctive affirmative, categorical not particular negative) are met. Write the conclusion in the form 'Always, either some obligatory acts are acts of prayer or some recommended acts are acts of prayer.'
?
Identify which of the six conjunctions would be used if the categorical premise were 'Nothing of an act of prayer is a revealed law' and the disjunctive premise were 'Always, either every revealed law is obligatory or every revealed law is recommended.' (Answer: the fifth or sixth conjunction, depending on the quality of the disjuncts).

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The First Conjunction: A Worked Example of a Fourth Figure Compound Syllogism
Author enumerates valid conjunctions of disjunctive and categorical propositions in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a fourth figure compound syllogism when both premises are universal affirmative.

Core Idea
The first conjunction produces a universal affirmative disjunctive conclusion with particular affirmative parts from two universal affirmative premises.

The first conjunction is the simplest valid combination in the fourth figure: a disjunctive premise with two universal affirmative disjuncts and a universal affirmative categorical premise. The author of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq shows how the conclusion follows: from each disjunct combined with the categorical premise, we derive a particular affirmative proposition, and then combine them into a disjunction. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
From the text: Disjunctive premise: 'Always, either every B is J or every B is D.' Categorical premise: 'Every A is B.' Recognize that both are universal affirmative (A-type). The middle term is B.
The disjunctive is universal because of 'دائماً' (always).
2
Check the conditions for validity
The disjunctive is affirmative (إيجاب المنفصلة). The categorical is not particular negative. Since the categorical is universal affirmative, no further restriction from the second condition. The conjunction is one of the productive ones.
If the categorical were particular negative, it would be invalid.
3
Derive the conclusion from the first disjunct
Take the first disjunct 'every B is J' and the categorical 'every A is B'. This is a categorical syllogism: all B are J, all A are B, therefore all A are J (Barbara). But the text says 'some J is A'. This is the converse of 'some A is J', which follows from 'all A are J' by subalternation (since 'all A are J' implies 'some A is J'), and then conversion yields 'some J is A'. So the intermediate conclusion is 'some J is A'.
The text skips the step of 'all A is J' and directly gives 'some J is A' due to immediate inferences.
4
Derive the conclusion from the second disjunct
Similarly, from 'every B is D' and 'every A is B', we get 'some D is A' via the same reasoning.
5
Form the final disjunctive conclusion
Combine the two particular affirmatives into a disjunctive proposition: 'Always, either some J is A or some D is A.' The text states that this disjunction is of the type مانعة الخلو دون الجمع, which means it is exclusive: at least one is true, possibly both. This is because the disjuncts are not necessarily mutually exclusive.
The whole conclusion is universal affirmative (دائماً) with the disjunction operator, making it a universal affirmative disjunctive.

In the first conjunction, what is the conclusion when the disjunctive premise is 'Always, either every B is J or every B is D' and the categorical premise is 'Every A is B'?

The Syllogism

Eight Conjunctions of Disjunctive-Categorical Syllogisms
Author enumerates eight conjunctions of disjunctive-categorical syllogisms with their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion form of any disjunctive-categorical syllogism given the type of disjunction and the quantity/quality of its parts.

Core Idea
Eight conjunctions, eight conclusions: the pattern depends solely on the parts of the disjunction.

This passage systematically presents eight forms of syllogisms that combine a disjunctive major premise with a categorical minor. The disjunction is the inclusive type 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' (at least one true, possibly both). Each conjunction varies the quantity and quality of the disjunct parts, producing a predictable conclusion. Mastering these forms equips you to handle complex compound arguments in Islamic logic, where mixed premises are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Note that the disjunction is 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' – inclusive disjunction (at least one true, possibility of both). This is the base for all eight forms.
2
Read the disjunct parts (أجزاء الانفصال)
Each conjunction specifies whether the parts are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. For example, in the first conjunction the parts are particular affirmative: 'some B is J' and 'some B is D'.
The parts are about the middle term B and the two extremes J and D.
3
Apply the categorical minor premise
The minor premise is always a categorical proposition affirming that كل أ ب (Every A is B). This links the subject A to the middle term B.
4
Derive the conclusion pattern
Using the given reasoning in the passage, deduce the conclusion. For instance, with particular affirmative parts and the universal affirmative minor, the conclusion is a disjunction of the same form: 'some J is A or some D is A'.
The author provides brief justifications; study these to understand the logical steps.
5
Compare the eight conjunctions
Notice how changing the quantity/quality of the disjunct parts shifts the conclusion: some yield universal conclusions, others particular; some affirmative, others negative. This systematic variation is the key insight.
The pattern is consistent: the conclusion's disjuncts are transformed versions of the original parts.
